topics. All standpoints are included, ortho-
dox and heterodox ; and though the volumes
vary very much in value, nearly all are well
worth perusal. Bound in red cloth and well
printed. Price 3s, unless otherwise stated.

The Cape Kaffir won’t work to the satis-
faction of the foreign exploiters. Being pos-
sessed of large reservations he can live easily by
working a few hours per day, and consequently
declines to slave for the European. Ceoil Rhodes
is, accordingly, introducing the “ Glen Grey Land
and Labour Bill,” by which Kaffir reservations
held in common will be broken up into patches of
six morgen per family—said patches to be strictly
entailed. Now, no decent-sized family can pos-
sibly live on six morgen ; consequently, certain of
its members must be thrown on the labour market
and made to compete for a living. By such gentle
gradations is the heathen lifted higher and higher
in the scale of civilisation.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
19 of this issue.
Dear Bulletin, —You, who are indisposed
to acknowledge humour in modern Hinglish
writers or actors, should pity them for having
inherited the fiction that they are “ funny ” from
ignorant ancestors. Congreve wrote, “ I look
upon humour to be almost of English growth ; at
least, it does not seem to have found such increase
on any other soil. And what appears to me to be
the reason of it is the great Freedom, Privilege,
and Liberty which the Common People of England
enjoy. Any man that has a humour is under no
restraint for fear of giving it vent; they have a
proverb among them which, may be, will show the
bent and genuis of the People, as well as a longer
discourse. He that will have a May Pole, shall
have a May Pole. This is a maxim with them and
their Practice is agreeable to it. I believe some-
thing considerable, too, may be ascribed to their
feeding so much on flesh, and the grossness of
their Diet in general.” If there is, or was, any-
thing in Congreve’s theory, Young Australia
must be laying the foundation of a glorious
national humour. —Yours, Meat.

According to showman Talmage there will
be three women to one man in Heaven. Which
prospect certainly does offer a fellow some induce-
ment to put 6d. into the plate instead of 3d.
Tamworth (N.S.W.) Observer, re a recent
tragedy: “Wallis was shot in the neck, Lane
was shot behind the ear, and Bateman shot him-
self in the verandah of the accommodation house.”
The average Freemason’s fraternal life is
set down at eight years. Hedies, gives up the
order, falls into monetary arrears, or in some
other way drops out of active service in that time.
Geo. Black, M.L.A. (N.S.W. ), who has
already one political work in the English press—is
now revising and amplifying his admirable essays,
“Organisations of the Discontented” (first pub-
lished in The Bulletin), with a view to shortly
issuing them in England in book-form.
Dear Bulletin, — However right “Binghi”
may be about the non-combativeness of Australians
generally, the Port Phillip wood-carters furnish —
or used to—a notable exception. It was once my
fortune to reside at a pub. near Dandenong, at
which these gentlemen used to settle their differ-
ences, and there was hardly a Saturday afternoon
that didn’t see three or four secundum artem
“ finish ’’-fights. Effusion of beer came first, then
of blood, then swankey again, and more gore, till,
when tanglefoot got finally on top, the paladins
would depart together in a high state of brotherly
love and bash. —A.M.
The English Radical press say Gladstone
should be gagged if he can’t keep his tongue quiet.
The gay ex-Premier persists in showing that
there’s life in the old dog yet, and has been utter-
ing opinions on the education question, and
the liquor question, and various other ques-
tions, quite at variance with the professions
and policy of the great Liberal party. Glad-
stone, in fact, declines to stay on his shelf.
He was born talking; he has lived talking ; and
he will die talking. The chances are that far
away in the 1900’s he will still be flooding the
country with dogmatic post-cards, impressions of
new books, defences of Genesis, and remarks that
the L.P. isn’t half what it used to be. And the
older and greyer and more drivelling he gets the
more the public will revere him, and the more
impotently the young lions of politics will wail and
curse as he spins interminable verbiage into
illimitable platitudes. The Liberal party has
lived for a long time on Gladstone’s tongue; it
wouldn’t be at all surprising if it died of it.

Dear Bulletin, —How’s this (fact) for a
tight place ? On many cattle-stations in G. and
N.Q. it is customary to destroy scrub cattle either
by shooting or by ripping them up. In the latter
case the operator rides up alongside the beast,
both often going at full gallop. Then the rider
stoops down, and with his knife makes a sudden
deep cut near the beast’s flank, disembowelling
him. The knife is brought from under the belly
up, and one cut suffices, the animal dying a
lingering death. For this brutal business both
nerve and quickness are required. One day a
young fellow, barely 21, named C , was out
with others on a station in Clermont (Q.) district.
He “took after” a scrub bull, intending to rip
him. He had his knife ready and was just stoop-
ing to make the cut when the bull swerved in
front of the horse—a common trick with beasts
when pressed. The collision resulted in horse,
rider and bull falling together, with the loins of
the bull pinning the man by his leg. Young
C had kept hold of his opened knife, and at
once ripped up the bull. The bull rose imme-
diately, and 0 got up, caught his horse,
remounted, and went on with his work as if
nothing had happened.—M. Rule.
“ Our existing system is not, as people
hypocritically pretend, a system of pure mono-
gamous marriage ; it is a mixed system of marriage
and prostitution, or rather, if one treats it from
the practical side in the order in which most men
come to know it, a mixed system of prostitution
and marriage. The greater number of men are
introduced to the sexual life through prostitution
alone ; they bring at last to marriage and the pro-
duction of future generations only the leavings
and relics of an effete constitution. Our whole
existing social fabric is based upon the degra-
dation of the paid harlot—that is to say,
upon the vile slavery of a large number
of unhappy women ; and it also involves other and
still more soul-killing practices on the part of a
vast proportion of our developing boys. Hardly
one man in ten brings to marriage and child-
getting an unimpaired virility. It will be the
object of the New Hedonism to combat these vile
vices ; to put the relation of the sexes and the pro-
duction of children on a sound and wholesome
basis, moral, physical, and emotional; to insist on
the rights of unborn and as yet unbegotten genera-
tions. Hedonists will not rest till they have re-
lieved the women of the community from the
hateful slavery of the streets, till they have vindi-
cated the claim of the children of the community to
a sound father and a sound mother. They will
not rest till prostitution is as effectually dead for
our race as polygamy ; till the equal freedom and
dignity of woman is universally admitted. What
they ask is that every man and every woman shall
live a life of perfect purity and perfect liberty ;
that every child shall be the pure offspring of a
healthy and natural union of unmixed affection.
No bought love; no forced cohabitation with a
drunken, a violent, or a distasteful husband, for
the production of hereditarily-tainted children;no
priestly blessing on a wicked bargain between
the unfit, the decrepit, the insane, the cancerous.
The New Hedonism will protest against all that,
and will cry out with a trumpet voice, in spite of
detraction, that whatever makes for race-preserva-
tion is pure and holy, whatever makes for race-
extinction or race-degradation is vile and hateful;
and it will hold it vile and hateful still, though
the Archbishop of Canterbury stand over to
invoke the benediction of God on a loveless mar-
riage or an immoral compact.”—Grant Allen, in
The Humanitarian.

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian province, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish to
interfere.

to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.

Archibald.

“ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 24, 1894.
Two Land-Tax Bills.
New South Wales and Victoria have simul-
taneously decided, subject to the approval of
an unfriendly Legislative Council, upon the
adoption of a Land and Income-tax. The
decision has been arrived at in different
ways, but in each case it is expressive of the
growing tendency towards direct taxation,
and the desire to make wealth bear a larger
share than heretofore of the public burdens.
In Victoria the Land-tax policy is not en-
tirely new. A small and inefficient impost
of a somewhat similar nature has long been
one of the minor sources of the public
revenue, and an additional Land-tax has
been levied by local Government bodies for
purely local purposes, but Premier Turner
proposes to largely increase the duty as a
means of meeting the deficiency which has
afflicted the province ever since the days of the
boom and the Coalition. In N. S. Wales it is
an entirely new device, land in that province
having been hitherto regarded as something
too sacred for taxation. Victoria proposes
to establish the new system as a matter of
necessity, and mainly for the purposes of
revenue. New South Wales does it in order
that it may dispense with a few of the exist-
ing Customs-duties, mainly those upon im-
portedluxuries—which dutieshave been found
guilty of encouraging local industries, and
furnishing the miserable Cornstalk inhabitant
with employment at the expense of the
foreigner. In New South Wales the Foreign-
trade party professes itself the Land-tax
party par excellence , and claims that Foreign-
trade and Land-tax are convertible terms.
In Victoria the lie is given to this blatant
issumptiou by the fact that it was the Pro-
tectionist party which first established the
tax, and it is the same party which now pro-
poses to increase it against the bitter oppo-
nition of the Argus —the great organ of
Freetrade in the South. Also, Premier
Turner introduces the Land-tax as an ex-
pedient, which it is; while Premier Reid
foolishly tries to elevate it to the dignity of
a gospel, which it certainly is not.
• • «
It is partly out of this difference of
sentiment that the chief discrepancy between
the two schemes arises. In each case
it is proposed to levy a tax of Id. in
the £ on unimproved values, but whereas
the Victorian measure allows an exemption
up to £lOO value, the New South Wales
proposal makes no such allowance. Assum-
ing the average value of agricultural land, in
its natural state, to be £2 per acre, the Vic-
torian farmer with 50 acres will go entirely
free ; the man with 100 acres will pay on 50
only ; the man with 150 acres on 100 only,
and so on. The obvious result is that there
will be a direct inducement to sell land in
small allotments and to keep it in a
state of subdivision. A farm of 50 acres,
with no land-tax upon it, should be worth, in
the open market, nearly 10 per cent, more
per acre than one of 20 times the size, of
which 900 acres are taxable. As a result the
tendency will be to create a multitude of
small farmers, by which system the produce
of the soil is vastly increased, it is made to
support a much larger population, and the
profits arising from the unearned increment,
instead of enriching a mere handful of huge
monopolists, are diffused throughout the
community. Also, it will create small inde-
pendent landholders rather than tenants
paying rent to landlords and corporations ;
and the fact that the mortgagee is taxed on
the same basis as the nominal landlord will
do something, at all events, to discourage
the curse of foreign capital which hangs so
heavily upon Australia.
* * #
Unluckily, Premier Turner, owing to want
of earnestness or some other cause, fails to
carry his own policy to its logical conclusion.
The £lOO exemption at one end of the scale,
and an additional impost of one-fifth of a
penny on the wealthy absentee at the other,
are an acknowledgment that there are land-
holders who are a benefit, and landholders
who are a curse, to the community at large,
but no sufficient distinction is drawn between
the two classes. The exemption, in the case
of the small landowner, is too small, and the
additional tax on the large one is not nearly
large enough. If the small landholder were
exempt up to £2OO of unimproved value,
and the tax were graduated up to 2d. or 3d.
in the £ on especially large estates, and the
impost on the absentee holder were increased
by 50 instead of 20 per cent., the amount
realised would be probably larger than
under the system now proposed, and the
benefit to the country would be incal-
culably greater. But politics are mainly a
matter of expediency, and Mr. Turner —in
a much greater degree than Mr. Reid —is
not his own master. Each Premier has to
reckon with a bitterly hostile Upper House,
but, whereas the New South Wales Council
can be swamped, if needful, by the appoint-
ment of new nominees, that of Victoria rests
on a property suffrage, and can be coerced
by no legal or constitutional means what-
ever. Therefore, Patterson’s successor has
probably carried his scheme of reform as far
as he can wioh any hope of success. The
present Land and Income Tax Bill may get
through the Chamber of Property ; a more
radical one would probably be hopeless from
the outset.
Premier Reid, with much greater oppor-
tunities, attempts oven less than his Victorian
contemporary. His bill contains the same
provision for a microscopic increase in the
case of the absentee, but it omits even Mr.
Turner’s scanty measure of exemption. Mr.
Reid has frequently acknowledged that the
small farmer should be encouraged in every
possible way, and that the large landholder
is a serious danger to the community, but in
the true Freetrade spirit he proposes to dis-
courage them both alike. The agriculturist
who makes a living out of £lOO or £2OO
worth of land is, in most cases, a miserably
poor and toilworn individual—about the
poorest and most toilworn in the community
—and the proposal to levy what is openly
described as a “wealth-tax” upon such
a person is unspeakably incongruous, but
even this aspect of the case cannot
prevail against solid Foreign-trade principles.
No provision is made for laying any special
disabilities even upon the largest land-
holders. Consequently, no inducements are
offered to them to sell their big estates, in
allotments, thus creating a class of small
land-holding agriculturists in place of tenants,
and dividing the unearned increment of the
future among a multitude of small properties
instead of using it to build up one colossal
fortune. The imperfections of Premier
Turner’s measure may be excused on the
ground that the Land Tax Bill must be
passed to save the Treasury from bank-
ruptcy, and it is necessary to present it in a
shape which will give it a fair show to get
through' the obstructive Upper House. But
Premier Reid is in no need of revenue, and
no special disaster will happen even if his
reform is temporarily delayed, therefore, the
dismally imperfect character of his proposals
is unaccountable.
* • »
Apparently Mr. Reid travels on the as-
sumption that the proposed impost is not an
expedient, but a cardinal virtue. As far as
can be gathered, he proposes to tax land, not
because it is accumulated wealth, for it is to
be taxed even in those cases where it is only
accumulated poverty ; nor with the purpose
of creating a race of small proprietary
farmers and diminishing the evils of land-
lordism, for the man who lets his land and
lives on the rent is placed on exactly the
same footing as the one who cultivates his
own holding ; nor witli a view to breaking
up big estates ; nor to raise revenue, for
revenue is not needed ; nor for any other
definite purpose whatever. He merely
does it because, according to a hazy
Single-tax theory, there is a certain virtue
in taxing land for its own sake, even
if no one is made any better or any
happier or any richer by the process.
Mr. Reid’s land-tax scheme, if it becomes
law, even with all its imperfections, will be
one of the few good things which has hap-
pened to New South Wales during the pre-
sent generation. But, all the same, it is a
miserably inadequate device, and its best
feature is that it will probably pave the way
for something better at a future date.
The Pack in Full Cry.
The passage of Australian Judges per ardua ad
alia —through the slough of Democratic society
to the holy ground of Privy Councildom—there
to
Sit and sip their nectar, while their bolts are hurled
Par below them in the valleys—
has always been a favorite dream of the Aus-
tralian “profession.” It has ever been felt that
the high capacity for cringing and tuft-hunting
wdiieh so many Australian lawyers display has
no fair field for its exercise under the Southern
Cross, where the rude blast of popular contempt
is apt to wither in its rathe and tender bloom
the fine flower of legal snobbery. This dormant
sense of wasted talent has been recently trans-
formed into a poignant and audible grief, and
the perplexed public which has not noticed the
pulling of the strings finds itself suddenly con-
fronted with the spectacle of an afflicted Bar
noisily piping its eye in the newspapers, while
a sympathetic Bench doubtless stands behind
to offer “moral support ” and handkerchiefs.
* * *
Fully to benefit by the many excellent morals
which this situation teaches, one should com-
prehend the force of the celebrated legal
maxim declaring that a door must be either
open or shut (Martin, 0.J., Brass v. Brass,
361 N.S.W. L.R.). To place an Australian Judge
on the Privy Council is to open another door to
preferment for Australian lawyers. This con-
clusion has been pithily summed up in the well-
known apothegm, “ Billet—fill it 1” (Stephen,
0.J., Catchem v. Chetum, 594 N.S.W. L.R.).
If a New South Wales Judge goes to England,
the whole of the New’ South Wales legal pro-
fession gets a leg up. A senior Q.C. becomes
Judge ; a junior Q.C. becomes a senior Q.C. ; a
stuff' gown is turned into silk ; and so on all the
way down to the briefless, hungry fledgling
wdio attends court only to act as claqueur
for ’Sonali’s w r eak jokes. The sequence
is logical as well as legal. Australian
Judge on Privy Council—door open —pro-
motion — Joy. No Australian Judge on
Privy Council —door shut—stagnation—Gloom.
This doctrine has been conclusively laid down
by the highest authorities (Cockburn, C.J.,
Spider v. Fly, 1033 L.R.).
« « •
Now, Judge Windeyer has nearly or quite
served the terra for which he was appointed to
N.S. Wales Supreme Court Bench, and the
problem which is exercising the N.S. Wales legal
profession is, What is to become of Judge
Windeyer ? He is still hale and vigorous ; he
is one of the best lawyers in Australia, as he is
one of the worst Judges ; he will hardly yet be
content to retire into private life. As he has
frequently had occasion to comment on the
ignorance of Australian matters shown by Privy
Council Judges, why should he not go to
England to spend his declining years in draw-
ing closer “those silken ties, which,” etc., and
perchance to transmit to posterity the record
of a grateful sovereign’s appreciation of the
services to throne and altar rendered by Baron
Windeyer, of Mount Rennie ?
• * *
There is no reason for believing the Judge
himself to be averse to the course indicated, as
he has little inducement to undertake a second
term of office in N.S.W. on the chance of becom-
ing Chief Justice. The present occupant of that
position, Mr. Frederick Darley, is a sturdy
Roman-father personage whose vigorous and
stately demarche is one of the sights of Sydney,
and who looks likely, bar accidents, to outlive
all his colleagues. Thus, if Judge Windeyer
were asked to-morrow, would he go to the
Eden above, it is all Maoriland to a moa that
he would reply with a ringing “ Right-oh !”
And the “profession,” as already explained, is
most anxious to shove him—just as the Queens-
land profession is anxious to shove Chief Justice
Griffith on to the Privy Council Bench as
Baron Griffith, of Kanakaland.
• » •
So, a few weeks ago, a timid little corres-
pondent wrote from Sydney to London Times,
and pointed out how desirable it was, in the
interest of Imperial unity, that some encourage-
ment should be given to loyal Australians
hungering to take their disputes to the other
end of the earth for decision. And the Times
said, “ Why, cert’n’ly!” and bellowed full-
mouthed support of the plain proposition that
Australian affairs should undoubtedly be
directed from London. And the imperialistic
papers, and what is left of the gang which ran
the insolvent Imperial Institute, took up the
cry, and rejoiced in this new proof that Aus-
tralia yearned to sacrifice herself for the benefit
of “the dear old mother country,” and the
Home News went so far as to congratulate its
readers that
There are discernible in the aspirations of the colonists
a note of Imperialism and a desire to share in the central
work of the great British family, w hich are very gratifying
at a time when—&&, &c.
The Home News went on to express the hope
that “ the matter will not be permitted to drop
into oblivion for the want of properly-organised
agitation.” And the wire-pullers in Australia
read and chuckled.
* * *
But, as Australians are quite indifferent to
the advantages of paying an extra judge a
high salary to go to England and decide
what shall be law in Australia, it is con-
sidered time something was done to work
up the agony here. The S.M. Herald ia
hand and glove with the lawyers ; and its sup-
port is secured. So the business of manufac-
turing local “public opinion” is rapidly
proceeding. Barrister 0. G. Heydon writes to
point out the enormous benefit which would
accrue to Australian suitors by taking their
cases regularly to London to be tried—what
glorious expenses there would be, how the
health of witnesses would be benefited by the
trip, how indefinitely legal costs would be piled
up, and how much more satisfactory would be
the decisions of an Australian Judge sitting in
London than the decisions of the same Judge
sitting in Australia. Barrister F. E. Rogers
(who threw up .£2OOO a year as Land Judge in
a huff because he could not get the exact
Supreme Court status and pension, and has, one
would imagine, been mighty sorry ever since)
writes to compliment barrister Heydon on his
“very able” letter, and thinks the Government
should take the matter in hand and open that
door at once. Barrister Robert D. Pring
writes to say that he has been struck in an
inexpressibly tender spot by the brilliant argu-
meuts of barrister Heydon and barrister
Rogers, that he cannot find words to
say how much he admires their noble notions,
and that magnet est veritas et prevalehit. And
presently the Herald will rise to point out the
singular unanimity of opinion which charac-
terises this correspondence, and to urge the
Legislature at once to proceed to the work of
securing the large profits which are waiting to
be plucked by the legal profession—no!—beg
pardon !—by “the country.”
# * •
Then some lawyer will move the adjournment
of the Legislative Assembly to draw attention
to the urgency of this great question, and
all .the other lawyers will listen gravely, and
will rise one after the other to affirm that they
have long thought that the highest interests of
Australia demanded that a judge should be
sent to the Privy Council, and it is a mystery
to them how Australia has managed to get along
so far without this indispensable stimulus to
national growth. And the lawyer-Premier will
be much impressed ; and, if it w'asn’t for the
Labor-party, The Bulletin really believes the
little job might be consummated.
* * *
So hyke on, good dogs ! Hark to their
music ! See that cunning old hound, laboring
doggedly just behind the leaders. He knows
too much to waste his breath in yelping ; he
leaves that to the younger ones. But The
Bulletin wagers he’ll be first in at the death.
Yoicks ! Tally-ho !
The Wairarapa Wreck.
Further evidence so far only serves to upset
all previous theories concerning the causes of
the Wairarapa tragedy. Captain MTntosh,
according to all available information, had been
noted throughout his career as a splendid navi-
gator, and a man of more than ordinary steadi-
ness, nerve and determination. Yet on this
occasion he seems to have set his vessel’s course
loosely, gone ahead at full speed on a rough and
foggy night through a dangerous archipelago,
and flatly refused, despite the suggestions of Ins
officers, either to slacken speed, to take sound-
ings, to blow the foghorn, or to use any precau-
tions whatsoever. Buch conduct on the part of
such a man can best be accounted for by ascrib-
ing it to one of those unsuspected mental kinks
on which are founded the great tragedies of the
world. It seems, to all intents and purposes,
a parallel case to that of Tkyon. Tha t unlucky
Admiral, alter a long and honorable career,
during which he was noted as a more than
usually efficient officer, deliberately smashed
his vessel in open daylight by peisisting
in an impossible manoeuvre, refused to
listen to the remonstrances of his subordinates
even at the last moment, declined to accept
assistance after the disaster, and finally went
down with his battered ship, a victim, appar-
ently, to a sudden derangement of the reason-
ing faculties. It does not seem, either in his
case or in that of MTntosh, to have been any-
thing resembling what is commonly known as
insanity. It looks rather like a sudden inertia—
a clouding of the mental eyesight—a general
slowing-down of the brain, which came upon
the patient after he had started on a dangerous
course, and caused him to let things slide.
Each man seemed overtaken with a torpor,
vaguely suggestive of opium or somnambulism
or partial catalepsy or aggravated fatalism,
which made him wait, in a calmly fatuous
fashion, for the catastrophe, doing nothing, and
caring very little, to avert it.
« * •
There are so many strange turns and twists
in the human mind, and so many undeveloped
possibilities lurking among the convolutions of
the brain, that no one can tell—possibly no
one will ever be able to tell —how such things
arise. Who knows for certain whether he is
mad or sane, or whether any other man is mad
or sane, or whether he or any other man is, or
is not, capable of developing insanity at any
moment ? Almost every man, at some period
or other in his life, has done something w eirdly
and unaccountably foolish, not knowing before-
hand that he was going to do it, and not know-
ing a moment afterwards why he had done it.
The result is set down by others to all manner
of stereotyped causes—drink, opium, chloral,
original sin, mental abstraction, nerve-exhaus-
tion, liver, indigestion, temporary insanity,
spiritual influences and possession by the devil.
It often happens that no one among all these
and many other explanations suits the case.
Just now “the after-affects of influenza” is
the stereotyped explanation of almost every-
thing which is otherwise unaccountable. Three
hundred years ago it w'as witchcraft. A cen-
tury hence it may be mesmerism, the influence
of elementary spirits—anything. Meanwhile,
the law seldom worries itself about scientific
or pseudo-scientific theories. A stereotyped
jury of cheesemongers and butchers and tailors
sits upon the case and inspects it from a strictly
meat and slop-clothing standpoint. The twelve
men in the box examine the accused’s state o
mind as if it were a dubious sample of cheese


and weigh it as if it were steak, and measure it
•with the yardstick, and return a verdict
accordingly. Their deepest research, heyoud
the scales and the yardstick, consists in asking
if anyone smelt the breath of the accused just
before the accident and noticed evidences of
drink. If there were no signs of drink about
his breath the question of his mental condition
is held to be settled, and the S.M. Herald has
a leader on the subject showing that this
species of recklessness must be* 1 put down.”
m * -e- •
'There is somewhere in the human brain a
flaw whereby it comes to pass that a simple
action which a man has to do hundreds of thou-
sands of times until it becomes so absolutely
mechanical that it seems beyond all possibility
of mistake, is always done wrongly at last.
There is another flaw whereby the thing that is
seen constantly may become so familiar that at
last it is seen even when it is not there. There
is likewise an unlimited possibility for not
seeing the thing that is there, or for being
unable to realise the nature of the situation
which has been met with so often as to become
almost a portion of oneself. The engine-driver
■who has started his locomotive thousands of
times to the dropping of the same signal, at
last, from pure force of habit, sees it drop in
his stereotyped imagination, and goes ahead,
and is wrecked. The man who has stepped
thousands of times into the same lift generally
■ends by stepping into an imaginary lift when
the real one is absent, and falls to the bottom.
When a drama has an unusually long run, an
;actor not unfrequently ends by forgetting his
part, to all appearance simply through incessant
iteration. People see ghosts, not only of
human beings but of inanimate things, and
receive spirit - messages, and hear voices,
and fall down and break their necks over
shadows or over objects that are not present.
Ocher people get up unexpectedly and cut their
throats, and it is duly explained at the inquest
that the deceased w T as well and happy right up*
to the moment of the “rash act,” and had no
imaginable cause for his sudden exit from this best
of all possible worlds. Others again suddenly
quit a happy home and abandon a prosperous
career, and go out to heal diseases with breath-
ings and red flannels. The world is full of
cranks, temporary or permanent—so full that
even every sane man has his turn at being a
crank some time or other in the course of his
life. If the disease comes on him at a moment
of grave responsibility, then he is court-mar-
tialled for cowardice, or his certificate is sus-
pended and a jury of cheesemongers and
butchers and tailors returns a verdict of man-
slaughter. If it comes on him at some other
time he probably writes poetry until he re-
covers, or invents a flying-machine.
Sometimes this curious kind of mental dis-
ability rests on a discoverable basis. A clot
of blood no larger than a pea may tem-
porarily obstruct the action of the brain,
and reduce the brightest intellect to torpid
stupidity. Sometimes no material cause what-
ever can be discovered. The imaginative
Celts of northern Britain recognised the exist-
ence of a state in which an otherwise sane man
goes recklessly and unaccountably to his
destruction with a light heart and an absolute
imperviousness to counsel, and described him
as “fey,” i.e., doomed. The (alleged) Anglo-
Saxon, who is not imaginative, does not recog-
nise such a state, and merely wants to know if
the deceased smelt of drink. Finding that he
didn’t, the Anglo-Saxon generally concludes
that he wasn’t smelt properly. The obvious
verdict on Captain MTntosh, of the Wairarapa,
will be that his ship was lost through utter-
recklessness and want of ordinary precaution.
But, whether he neglected all ordinary precau-
tion because his intellect temporarily stopped
working, as so many intellects do, sometimes with
visible cause and often without it; or whether
he had seen the open waters of the Hauraki
Gulf so often that at last he saw open water
where there was none; or whether he had
learned the art of navigation so well that he
suddenly forgot all about it it; or whether his
time and that of his ship had both come, and
they simply went ahead and fulfilled their
destiny according to that good old theory of
predestination which is brother to fatalism, no
tribunal can ever authoritatively decide. There
are things on which even twelve sober trades-
men who have been got together in a room and
sworn on an unclean Bible can throw no light
whatever. And one of them is the fact that
the best intellect has sudden stoppages and
breakdowns, and walks at times in its sleep,
and bolts at other times like that thoroughly
reliable horse which always does bolt sooner or
later. No penalty ever invented can remedy
this trouble. If the most methodical genius
on earth were entrusted with the simple
duty of calling round every day to move an
ash-barrel, and the whole universe were to stop
Tor ever if that barrel wasn’t moved, it would
still come to pass in the end that he would for-
get his duty and let the universe stop.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Indemnity for Trustees.
A Bill to extricate boomsters from a tight
place is passing quietly through the Victorian
Legislative Council. It is officially known as
the Trusts Act Amendment Bill. For some
time past scandalous disappearances of pro-
perty confided to trustees have been so fre-
quent and notorious that some amendment in
the acts of those protected persons was much
hankered aftef. Wherefore the late Solicitor-
General introduced the Bill aforesaid . during
last session. Its name was calculated to inspire
confidence, but when the Amendment Bill iiad
Jeeu further “amended” in Committee and
was about to become law the Patterson
Government got orders to cease legislating.
Mr. Cuthbert, the new Solicitor-General,
picked up the measure and brought it ag > u
before the Council last week, with a few cast"a!
remarks. Cuthbert, on the subject of clause
25, was interesting: “Only one or two slight
alterations had been made in the Bill as it
passed through Committee before. Clause 25
gave trustees an indemnity for lending on
mortgage upon the report of a valuer if
the loan was up to only three-fifths of the
value. An addition to that clause made it
retrospective . ” The addition is a little tiling
slipped in by Cdthbert. The former Solicitor-
General and parent of the bill (Wynne) pointed
out at once that the “ retrospective ” suggestion
was fully discussed last session, and rejected.
When the bill had undergone a second reading
for the second time, and been put into com-
mittee once more, Mr. Wynne attempted to
re-establish the original verdict on Clause 25
by moving that Mr. Cuthbert’s little addition
be expunged, but Sargood arose in his
ministerial might and brushed the protest aside
with a remark that “ if it is right now for
trustees to lend up to three-fifths of the valua-
tion on property it was surely right in the
past.” The same Council which wouldn’t
sanction a retrospective indemnity last session
was glad to swallow its scruples the other
night, and Cuthbert’s little extra was agreed
to. Clause 25, as it now stands, is full of com-
fort for anyone who devoted the widow and the
orphans’ cash to boom - promotion. Five
years ago, or even more recently, a man
was able to get a value of ,£5OOO put
upon a piece of ground that had cost him £5OO
the previous day. Then he went to his pal, the
trustee, and having winked thrice at that
responsible investor, procured a mortgage of
£3OOO upon the inflated property. In due
course the property reverted to its original
value, or less. The £3OOO robber generally
failed to pay up his interest, and the trustee
paid it himself rather than sell the land and
become liable for the difference between real
value and the boom “ loan.” Clause 25 relieves
him of his liability. The widow and the
orphans will have their claim upon their
financial protector wiped out by Act of Parlia-
ment. A few more years shall roll, and the
indemnified swindle of the old boom-time will
be the privileged popular swindle of another
boom time, thanks to clause 25. To the poor
victims, who find their money lost in a 3-sths
advance upon a valuation ten times greater
than it should have been, another Sargood will
explain that what was right in the past must
surely be right evermore.
The Registration Clause—Again!
According to the foolish registration clause in
the Reid Government’s new N.S.W. Land Bill,
a certain circuitous measure of protection from
seizure by creditors is afforded to the N.S.W.
selector, who registers bis homestead according
to certain elaborate forms and ceremonies which
no average selector is ever likely to understand.
The Bulletin has twice pointed out that the
protection ought to be afforded without regis-
tration, that the process ought to be automatic
and universal, and that if it is a good thing to
protect the agriculturist’s home from seizure for
debt it is a good thing to do it whether he is
registered or not. The following extract, from
the recently-published report of the S.A. In-
spector of Homesteads, shows how this paper’s
view of the matter has been upheld by experi-
ence elsewhere :
By clause 12 of the Blockholders’ Loans Act of 1891 pro-
vision was made for indorsing the leases of blockholders
to secure them from seizure for debts incurred after in-
dorsement, but from some cans • or causes this provision
has been availed of in Only a few instances, partly because
of a disinclination on the part of men suffering from mus-
cular weariness to do anything out of the usual routine,
and mainly from an inability to apprehend any immediate
benefit in attention to the matter or danger from neglect-
ing it. If the provisions clause were inserted in and made
part of the lease at the outset I believe it would be univer-
sally appreciated.
The moral is that it is not safe to leave too
much to individual initiative. Most men want
to be protected against themselves more than
against anybody else. Also, very few people
in any grade of life, and very few indeed among
the working classes, know anything in parti-
cular about law, and the most beneficent law
that was ever enacted generally remains for
many years a dead letter if its advantages are
purely optional. This applies to the registra-
tion idea in particular. Every man thinks
himself immortal until he dies, and no man
worries about his own prospective insolvency
until his debts become burdensome. A system
which enables him, when he is free from liabili-
ties, to secure his home against possible lia-
bilities in the future, doesn’t appeal to the
average citizen in the least. How many men
have the sense while prosperous to settle a
home on their families ?
Land-Tax and Land Resumption.
One of the principal advantages to be derived
from a land and property tax —as distinguished
from a lax on unimproved land-values only
is that it supplies an authentic valuation of all
the fixed property in the province, which valua-
tion is of the utmost importance when the
Government is resuming land for State pur-
poses. Hitherto there has been, in New South
Wales, no land-tax of any kind, and, conse-
quently, no authentic valuation whatsoever.
When a new railway or other public work is in
course of construction the owners whose pro-
perty is to be resumed almost always demand
twelve times, and generally get six or eight
times, its real worth. Almost every auctioneer,
valuator, estate agent, or other business man
in the province will rise up and perjure himself
on the subject for a reasonable fee, and as the
result New South Wales has already been
swindled out of (probably) £10,000,000 or so,
per medium of the Public Works Department,
and will be swindled out of many more
millions in the future if the present fraudu-
lent system is continued. And, so far as
appears up to date, it is to be continued.
The proposed tax will supply very little
information which will be of service ;
it will furnish an authentic record of unim-
proved values only, but the value of the im-
provements will still be estimated on the testi-
mony of the same old lying auctioneer, and the
country will be swindled in very much the same
old way as heretofore. And even if the Local
Government system provides for levying rates
upon lands and all improvements thereon, and,
consequently, for a valuation of all the lands
and improvements in the province, this also
will be of no service whatever, unless it is pro-
vided bv law that all resumption 0 -hall be made
on the basis of the Local Government valua-
tion, with a small percentage (10 per cent,
would be quite enough) added as compensation
for disturbance. Victoria has about the most
perfect Local Government system in Australia,
but, for want of this simple and logical proviso,
an owner is allowed to pay taxes on a value of
.£lOOO and yet to claim —in which case
he generally gets ,£SOO0 —for his property, when
it is required for railway or other public pur-
poses. And, according to all present appear-
ances, New South Wales is going to miss its
opportunity in precisely the same manner.
What is wanted is that there shall be one tax —
whether for local or central purposes doesn’t
matter—which shall be based on the value of
lauds and improvements, so as to provide the
necessary valuations, and that the State shall
have power to make any resumptions it may
think tit on the basis of these valuations, and
on them onty. A too close devotion to the
principle of taxation on unimproved values
alone will leave the land-resumption swindle
very much where it was before. At the best,
and provided special legislation is passed-to
that effect, it will prevent the perjured
valuator swearing quite so freely as here-
tofore about the value of the land itself,
but this will only cause him to perjure him-
self all the more concerning the improvements,
and the difference in the final result will be
comparatively small. Experience has shown
that there is no limit to the value which an un-
blushing expert will put upon a four-roomed
shanty, provided he is paid a reasonable fee for
doing it, and there is also no visible limit to the
amount which a jury will award when the State
has to pay. For some occult reason, when the
State has to pay, a jury’s sympathies are
always with the “resumed” proprietor, and if
it is precluded by law from awarding exorbi-
tant amounts as compensation for resumed land,
it will simply award the same compensation for
fences, buildings, crops, and damage to the
owner’s feelings through the rending of home
ties. Unless all such awards are fixed on an
automatic principle, the resumption swindle
has no end.
Any Australian Government which wants to do
some genuine retrenchment might begin by
abolishing the Postmaster-General. The Post-
office is a department which runs itself just as
effectually as does the Observatory, or the
Botanic Gardens, or the Government Printing
Department, and it no more requires a Cabinet
figure-head than any of them. It goes on in
the same business routine whether Protec-
tionists or Freetraders are in power; there
is no political principle involved in its
management any more than there is in the
Patents Office or the Barristers’ Admission Board,
No man’s political principles are of the slightest
use in the Post-office. The new Minister has
simply to start and learn the business or else
let it alone. If he lets it alone—most of them
do—there is no use for him. If he learns it he
is “ chucked out” just as he is beginning to
know something, and again there is no use for
him! If he lets it alone he wastes nobody’s
time and salary save his own ; if he insists on
learning things he wastes the valuable
time of the Deputy P.M.tr. In teaching
him. The Postmaster-Generalship is ixually
reserved for the acknowledged fool of
the Cabinet, because any fool can fill the billet
by simply staying away. If the billet and the
salary were dropped altogether, and the
Deputy-P.M.G. was left to run things—which
he does already—it would save a bleeding
country something like .£l5OO a year. Also, it
would save the necessity for having a fool in
the Cabinet. The position is so eminently
suited for a mild, unobtrusive dolt that its
existence makes it seem almost necessary to
find a dolt to fill it.
Dear Bulletin, —I am a Sydney man at pre-
sent visiting England. Naturally I went to the
Imperial Institute, and with a fellow-Austra-
lian eventually found myself in one of the
numerous refreshment-rooms. I carefully
scanned the wine-card, and, in the long list,
failed to find one single production of Australia.
On interrogating the waiter he said they had no
“ colonial” wines on sale. The fact speaks for
itself. Here is an Australian-subsidised insti-
tution whose professed object is to foster the
relationship between Great Britain and her
colonies and to boom the industries of the
latter, more especially when they do not
clash with those of the former country ; yet
while wines from all foreign parts can be
obtained there is no chance given to_ the
English public to taste the pure juice of the
grape produced by us. Neither are there any
Cape or other colonial wines on sale. This, in
spite of the fact that the Australian wines are
extensively advertised on all the railway-
stations and other places, and exhibits of them
can be seen in the Courts. In the N.S. Wales
section is a large painting, supposed to repre-
sent a view of Sydney harbor. A more
wretched picture I have rarely seen. It looks
as if the painter had done it from a small
photograph and trusted to his imagination to
fill in the details. The whole thing is a dis-
grace to the Court, and bears no resemblance to
the place it is supposed to represent. Surely
someone connected with the Institute lias seen
“ our beautiful harbor,” and must know that it
is not a picture of it ! —Yours truly, P. J.
Marks.
The success of the Maoriland Village Settle-
ment system, as compared with the failure of
the schemes adopted in N.S. Wales and Victoria,
clearly shows that the virtue of self-help is the
one which it is most important to cultivate
when attempting to “ settle the people on the
land.” The M.L. Village Settlement scheme,
or, as it is locally known, the “Small-farm
Association System,” is wholly individualistic
in principle, though voluntary co-operation is
practised and encouraged. The settler gets his
land on perpetual lease at a rental of 4 per cent,
on value, and the Govt, has power to make a
small advance to enable him to build a house.
Where possible, contracts for road-making and so
on are put in this way. It is found, however, that
the less money the Govt, advances, the better
it is for the settlers; and no advances whatever
were made to last year’s settlers. But the
essence of the system is that every man’s re-
ward is in exact proportion to his labor ; he
knows that what he earns is his own ; and the
vital spirit of independence which this plan
fosters must be adopted in Australia also, if
village-settlements are ever to get beyond the
status of a State-soup kitchen.
If it is desirable to prohibit the relatives of
Judges from pleading before those Judges to
whom they are kin, is it not advisable to pro-
hibit lawyers from appearing before coroners
and magistrates to whom they are blood-
related ? But the plumping of the pebble of
investigation into this pool of enquiry disturbs
never-ending circles. Many men—and judges,
magistrates and even bush-coroners are only
men—are more readily and powerfully swayed
by friends whose tendrils are mere sympathies
of sentiment, by business dominances which
exert a garotter’s choke, by money-lending
vampires of the Scrooge order, by fearsome
Mrs. Grundys that threaten to scrape off the
coatings from whited sepulchres, and by dozens
of irrational and distant influences, than by any
of the manly and natural predilections that
spring from the verity embosomed in the other-
wise inane proverb that “ blood is thicker than
water.” Yet it is because the natural, manly
and healthy affections are the more excusable,
and because the sinister influences are less
easily concealed when once suspected, that
public feeling resents the familied monopoly of
the law-courts and so-called courts of justice
by men whose social boast it is that they are
pedigreed in the science of living gorgeously on
human vices and of murdering truth and
debauching justice for commensurate fees; while
it is not greatly troubled by more sinister
influences, since these run risks specially their
own.
Japan thinks of taking, by way of indemnity,
the Chinese ironclad fleet, <£100,000,000, and
the island of Formosa, and stipulating that
Corea shall be declared independent. This, if
it comes off, ought to about finish the Chinese
bogy. China has lost Burmah, Annam, Ton-
quin, Siam, Hongkong, Corea, and great slabs
of Manchuria and Chinese Tartary, all within
half a century, so that, in point of area, about
one-third of the empire is already gone. And
now that the ghastly weakness of the whole
leprous house of cards is fully revealed by the
Japanese campaign, Russia, which has hitherto
held off from actual hostilities, will probably
annex a lot of sea-coast, and then the other
Powers will also take a bite apiece, and China
will crumble away all at once. Europe has
possessed herself successfully of America, Aus-
tralia, Africa, and most of Asia, and she must
finish the job sooner or later. For all China is
worth to humanity at large, it might as well be
sooner as later.
The quick-brained and mercurial B. R. Wise,
M.P., is unlikely ever to develope into a master-
ful political leader. Ringnosed long ago by
Parkes, he appears unable to escape from an
inexplicable hypnotism. Placed by accidental
luck in prominent confederation with Premier
Reid, he threw over his newer chief under ex-
cuses that fell short of reasons by their abject
childishness, and had not even the vicious
stamina of audacious “crams.” Then, alarmed
at the askanceness of the attitude of his own
supposed special party, and the feebleness of
the support accorded to his trembling rebel-
lion, he rushes back into the arms of his
brother for no cause greater than that there
was no “ strawberry-mark on his left arm.”
Next, becoming conscious that he has been pub-
licly playing the fool, he attempts to discount
the reconciliation by declaring that it was
merely an accidental incident leading to a better
understanding between Reid and himself.
And this unstable unit was once the unmatured
hope of the New South Wales democratic
party. The only pity is that a possible coali-
tion cannot be effectually strangled with a rope
of sand.
The latest shearing trouble has broken out in
Otago, M. L., where sundry pastoralists have fixed
up a new freedom-of-contract agreement, under
which “ the shearer is to forfeit half his wages
if he leaves or is dismissed before shearing is
finished.” As he can be dismissed at the option of
the pastoralist, for any reason, or for no reason at
all save that the employer “darned well pleases”
to have it so, this agreement enables the squat-
ter to swindle every out of half his
wages. The customary plea is advanced, of
course, that no squatter would so shamelessly
abuse his authority, but no one professes to
explain why he is striving so hard to get the
power to perpetrate a heartless swindle if he
doesn’t.mean to use it. When a man struggles
his hardest to fix things so that he can defraud
another man if he wants to, there is very little
satisfaction in his own unsupported assurance
that he wouldn’t defraud the other man on any
account.
Editor Bulletin, The progress ol demo-
cracy in Australia can hardly be said to be in
full way until legislation has authorised a
system of licences by means of which men in
want of land can enter and cultivate unused
land in the same way as under the “ miners’-
right ” system men can enter unused goldfields
land to seek for gold. Farmers’-rights are as
necessary for justice to the farmer as miners’-
rights —won at the cost of blood—were and are
to the miner. There is no principle of morality
or of law which justifies or opposes the one
which cannot be brought forward with respect to
the other. The landowner on paper has no more
right to “shepherd” an unused block, waiting
for a bu3'er, than the miner to “ shepherd” his.
Use is the only true test of title, and in mining
the principle is tentatively at work, and it may
be said successfully. The law of titles to land
is founded on feudal principles, evolved in
the atmosphere of an age so unlike ours as to be
an anonjaly now, and accordingly is unjust to
those living now. The law of mining titles is a
product of our own times, our own wants, has
been moulded in accordance with our own sur-
roundings, and accordingly has a foundation of
justice. If it’s wrong for the unemployed man
to cultivate a few acres of the squatter’s run
now„it was wrong for the poor miner to break
the soil and frighten the sheep of the Crown

SUNDRY SHOWS.
SYDNRV SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Her Majesty's ... “The Two Orphans.”
Lyceum “Tne Foundling.”
MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK,
Princess s Theatre “ Nitouche.”
Theatre Royal “Jack Sheppard.”
Bijou ... "Skipped.”
The “Two Orphans ” opened their sorrow-
ful career at Sydney Majesty’s on Saturday in a
manner to which no serious objection could be
taken. This drama of 18th-century Pans concerns
the fates of two sisters—one blind and lost, and
the other distracted with grief and found by the
police ; and of two brothers—one a feeble cripple,
who grinds scissors, and the other a hulking
loafer, who drinks, and otherwise walks in the
footsteps of his hanged father. The latter is, in
The Bulletin’s opinion, the finest character in
Mr. RijJnold’s extensive repertoire, and this paper
is prepared to sit down flat on its solemn affidavit
and attest that it doesn’t know anywhere an actor
who could fill the part Letter. Jacques’ cursings,
the things which lie throws at his lame brother,
his ferocious humor, the cheerful manner in which
he shakes the loose coppers out of his mother and
brother for drink, his beneficent schemes for
“stoushiag” the blind girl, his huge calves and
frowsy overcoat, his horrid guffaw, the vigor with
which he kicks the furniture immense distances,
and his ghonl-like agility, make him one of the
most complete, artistic and thoroughly interesting
hogs of this paper’s acquaintance. If Mr. Rignold
would only give up playing the hero for awhile
and take to a course of unmitigated villainy, there
would be a boom at Her Majesty’s. G. R. isn’t
nearly so good a hero as he imagines, and he is
several dozen times better as a villain than he is
aware of.
* * *
The blind girl’s part consists chiefly in being
dragged around, and Miss Athena Claudius com-
pares very feebly just here with the vanished
M aggie Knight. Also her sister. Miss Watts-
Phillips, calls up recollections of another
lost idol in the form of Lucia Harwood,
and suffers by the comparison. Scot Inglis,
as the knife-grinding brother, is only ob-
jectionable through his habit of spreading his
hand over his chest to express emotion ; when he
forgets this mannerism and proceeds, after a hard
fight, to disembowel G. Rignold with a dinner-
knife, which some customer gave him to sharpen—
and which that person will probably carve his
dinner with afterwards —he gives pleasure to all
save the disembowelled lessee. Mrs. Melville,
the mother of the fallen savage and the knife-
grinder, fills another beery part unobjectionably,
and the other 21 characters occupy the stage while
these people are out begging, or drinking, or being
arrested, or otherwise getting into trouble. A
chorus of ladies of loose character lends the drama
an up-to-date expression. The period is supposed
to be that of Louis XVI., at which time there
existed, among other grotesqueries, a chib, where
fashionable ladies whose characters wouldn’t bear
close inspection, used to meet at intervals and cane
each other as some amends for their sins. A good
deal of eccentricity may be excused in such an
epoch as that.
** * *
“ Hands across the Sea ” didn’t meet with
the fate that its loyal idiocy deserved, and was
allowed to go on hoisting the British Flag for two
weeks, whereas one would have been more than
enough. It comes to a well-deserved end on
Friday, and “ The Wearin’o’the Green,” a new
Irish drama, succeeds it. It is anticipated that
this production will throw some new lights upon
the praste and the dhirthy informer, and that
there will be a colleen in it. At all events, the
present scribe is going to attend with a view to
seeing these parties, and if there is no colleen his
best feelings will be outraged and he will probably
take an opportunity of saying so.

* * *
Though “ Predatoros” was first introduced
to the world at an Australian theatre, the world
is not likely to cultivate its acquaintance. Gov.
Robinson’s music is really no stronger, or less in.
sipid, than the libretto. For the most part, it
seems incapable of finding employment for a large
orchestra. On the two or three occasions when
the composition is bold and extensive, it sounds
reniembersome, and nut one humorous musi-
cal effect relieves the sentimental monotony
of Robinson from first to last. Nellie Stewart,
Florence Young, Elsie Cameron, Brownlow,
Tapley and Lauri gave “Predatoros” such a
chance as no other local production would be
likely to get. They knew their words and were
all in good voice alter a Sunday rest. Perhaps
they forgot the tail-end of an air sometimes, but,
if so, they guessed at it, and they couldn’t well
guess wrong. The end of a Robinsonian melody
casts its shadow before. You can feel it coming
on, like the fits you have been subject to from
childhood upwards. Also, the premonitory symp-
toms are drowsiness, and a desire to go out for
refreshments. Feminine sassiety slumbered freely
during the second act of “Predatoros,” but the
men had their little tonics at the intervals, and so
found strength for an outbreak of loyal applause
at the finish. There was a small patch of enthu-
siasm in the amphitheatre, too. Somebody had
evidently planted it aloft—such things never get
there of their own free-will.
Copies of the pictures selected for W. and
M.’s tableaux are freely displayed at Glen’s, on
Melbourne Block. So far from the naughtier
ones being smuggled out of the window when
Maw worm snorts (as suggested in last issue), the
very naughtiest of all are allowed to beguile
johnnies, with a hope that they (the j’s) will see
the figure-subjects faithfully reproduced in the
flesh. This is, unhappily, a delusion. Will-o’-the-
Wisp in the picture has nodings on, whereas in
the tableaux the young lady conceals a critical
portion of her sweet outline with an absurd wisp
of muslin, or something.. J. C. W., who is a
terror for propriety, was probably the author of
this inartistic anachronism. He’s really Will o’
the Wisp—d'yer notice it?
The girls themselves, feeling quite at ease and
decent in their tight coverings, don’t care for
these irrelevant cloths. The damsel cast for the
statue of Venus, when posed on the white pedestal,
at rehearsal, raised an objection to the yard or
two of sheeting which the manager required her
to hold bunched in front of her. She would have
preferred to be like the original statue, for where
was the harm—in tights? “I say,” enquired
Venus, “can’t I do without this nuisance of a
thing?” The lump of calico was a difficult
matter to keep motionless, perhaps, with breezes
blowing from the wings. Poor girl! she gets over
the trouble now by pressing it close against her
form, as though she were ci ying to warm herself.
“Nitouche” and the Living Pictures have
come to stay awhile at Melbourne Princess’s. The
convent angel who takes a wildly-impossible night
off is reckoned one of Miss Nellie Stewart’s hap-
piest efforts, and indeed she acts with so much
French vivacity that one might think she was
creating the part. The drummer’s song is a
mighty success, in spite of a little English trans-
lation. Miss Nellie makes it seem a sparkling
ditty, whereas the incident of a bass drummer
meeting the girl he was formerly engaged to is not
a little bit comic. The lame, misshapen yarn, with
its witless refrain, fetches an encore for the sake of
the incidental business, which the actress pre-
sumably brought from Paris, but, if she hadn’t
gripped the very spirit of the business, what a
silly Song of the Drum it would be ! Organist
Laud remains a loyal Briton throughout. He
even sneezes, and falls upon his head in Anglo-
Saxon, and, as he is not required to sing a song of
the “Poof !-Bltf ” pattern, his insular performance
gives general satisfaction.
The Tableaux have been accepted without
a struggle. They attract young and old of either
sox an 1 every shade of political opinion, but their
influence is chiefly felt by the bald-headed Free-
trader. It sometimes happens that thelightiug of
the statues is not quite satisfactory, and the faces
suffer accordingly. No fault can be found, how-
ever, with the right-hand figure in the “ Three
Graces’’ group. This young lady is diviuelv
lovoly throughout. Her costume consists of
nothing, just nothing at all, and she would seem
an almost too-striking novelty if the tail-end of
the middle Grace’s drapery didn’t casually screen
a few square inches of her fair sister from the
limelight. It is an artful arrangement, tempting
many people to book seats for the next evening in
the hope that the centre Grace will not allow her
bit of garment to do double duty again. Alas !
it is always hanging carelessly in exactly the same
way.
** * *
“ Morocco Bound ” has suddenly turned
up again, in Melbourne, at the Bijou. A crowded
house on Saturday, and an abundance of horse
laughter, promised a lively season for Elton,
Shine, and Co., in their new quarters. The show
is not reduced in size, although the charges of
admission average about 60 per cent, less than
the Princess’s tariff, on which terms “ Morocco
Bound ” is undoubtedly a very cheap line in
public entertainment. Numerous gags, suitable
to a popular-priced audience, were worked in by
Elton on the first night. The squire’s little jokes
fell like hail upon the docile person of his big
baritone son, who stood aside and smiled patiently
until the leading man had finished with him.
Perhaps the younger son, the fair, fragile young
man who has pined in the background thus far,
could also be turned to account by his stage
parent. The ballet improved on its previous
Melbourne performances by rolling head over
heels, but the best of lady can-can dancers (and
these are not of the very best, by any means) cut
tame figures compared with men in skirts.
* * *
In “Little Jack Sheppard,” at the Royal»
the he-girls turn somersaults with as much vigor
as neatness. The idea of male athletes in female

TO THE TABLEAUX VIVANXS.
Fair Nymph of the Cascade,
Our weary soul refreshing—
So beautifully-made—
No creases in thy fleshing.
Through glasses do we stare,
And with the naked eye :
Ah ! would that we were there
To pinch you on the—sly.
O, Psyche at the Well,
Our broken slumbers haunting—
Could mortal ’scape the spell
Of outlines so enchanting >
Upon onr homeward way
Alone: the vulgar street
We think of thee and say—
“ Won’t Cupid have a treat 1 ”
Sweet Fairy of the Moon,
Reclining on its crescent,
With such a girl to spoon
Existence would be pleasant.
O pictures framed in gold—
The price no matter what—
If ever you are sold
We’ll go and buy the lot.
At Poverty Point.

Eloise Juno’s pupils gave an entertainment at Melb.
Masonic Hall last week. A quiet, surprising Romeo and
Juliet went through the balcony scene with credit. “In
Honor Bound was played, a stout young man wdth a
shiny brow and fuzzy head personating Philip without
anV credit whatever. Said young man turned up in four
parts of the programme with the very same shiny brow'
and fuzzy head, and by the inscrutable decrees of Fate he
had a heroine to kiss in three out of the four dramatic
scenes put on, Juliet alone escaping. Miss Juno recited
a i alleged Australian poem about a back-blocks bore who
carried his sister s picture in his chest. This item, how-
ever, was in part atoned for bv the closing item—
“ Maggie Macfarlane”—a Scotch character sketch that
was a triumph in spite of all the aforesaid shiny brow and
fuzzy hair could do.

Not long ago, at a leading Australian theatre, a well-
k iowii prima-donna had a double in comic opera. She
had to make a quick change from the heroine dressed in
f male garb to a hoy. To accomplish this she wore
the boy ’s dress beneath her other costume. The moment
arrived for the change, which was made, and on to the
stage she bounced. Presently a roar of laughter came
from the audience, which, to her astonishment increased
in volume. Then she made the awful discovery that She
had neglected to remove her hustle and had returned to
the stage we iring it over the boy’s costume. The act was
never repeated.

Signora Link, late of Sydney, expressed much weariness
Of spirit over the routine of professional work. Signora
Link’s plans at that time were to visit the Chicago Exhi-
bition and the Falls of Niagara, to spend a few months in
Germany, and then to settle in New York, where she was
sure of getting operatic and concert engagements suffi-
cient to free her from the trammels of daily teaching.
Signora. Link, whose Australian operatic career as a
dramatic soprano was particularly brilliant, retired from
the lyric stage upon her marriage with Mr. William A.
Harney, of Brooklyn.

A Chinese opera co. is now travelling in N.. Queensland.
Every performance lasts a fortnight, and at the Towers
the local “lallikins” grew so tired that they stoned the
singers and brought one scene to a sad conclusion not
provided in the libretto. The Emperor of China and his
Court, a mandarin and his wives, a good fairy, a rebel,
and a lady of uncertain virtue, are among the characters,
and European appreciation of their efforts is heightened
by utter inability to distinguish which from t’other. The
Chinese, however, are highly delighted ; and the R. S.
Smythe of the co., a fat little Chinaman who is a great
favorite with the ladies, announces that he will tour Aus-
tralia with a much larger co. after the war.
A well-known Australian boniface who, years agone,
was a prominent actor and whose bigness of heart is well-
known, finds himself occasionally in awkward predica-
ments through his verbal mannerisms. “With pleasure,
deah boy, with pleashah,” was a favorite expression of
his. One day he called at the house of a fellow-actor to
inquire after the health of the latter’s wife who had been
ill. “ She is dead—died an hour ago, Cully,” answered
the widower. “ Come up stairs and look at her, old
fellow.” “Certainly, with pleasure, deah boy, with
pleashah,” was the reply.

During the boom-time there were few more
powerful names than that of a certain man whose name
it will be charitable not to mention. On the burst coming,
he had not the advantage of the later discovery by which
boomers could privately compromise at Jd. to 6d. in £,.
and he had to file. Nearly all his contemporary speculat-
ing friends, who hung out long enough, have paid their
fractional compos, and are big bugs again, but his action
in “ filing ” is regarded by them as so contemptible that
ne’er a one of them would come forward last week and'
find a matter of £l4 to prevent his being committed for
trial for alleged embezzlement. If he were to inform a
few former friends, now highly-respected citizens of Melb.,
that he proposed writing a history of Melbourne’s finan-
ciers, he could raise that £l4 a thousand times over as the
price of his silence.

An examination of Federal Bank officials is
being held in Melb. Insolvency Court, for no avowed pur-
pose. Perhaps it had to be held to satisfy the Law, in
which case the Law would seem to have waited as long as
possible with the hope that the directors intended to die
before their evidence was called for. The exam, has an
extraordinary interest for all who are not too sick to re-
member the tone of the presiding Judge Molesworth
when James Muuro, as an insolvent, applied to him for a
sixpenny certificate of release from his debts. The ex-
chairman of the Federal, as we remember, carried off his
certificate with flying colours. Judge Molesworth re-
marked that poor Jimmy’s failure was the result of pure
bad luck, and his Honor went out of his learned way to
grant the insolvent’s release with much pleasure.
Hear Judge Molesworth with reference to the
bank dealings of Munro’s co-director, William M‘Lean
“ False balance-sheets are most discreditable to people
who manufacture them. You could use a very ugly word
[by the way, judges don’t use ugly words except in
denouncing ill-dressed culprits] to describe this transac-
tion, for it is an ugly one. Some people who did such
things might be prosecuted as conspirators.” How tiring
is this talk of what might be done, but is never accom-
plished-in Melbourne 1 But why stir up another of the
old Melbourne stinks in order that a jury may call it otto
of roses ?
On the 31st March, 1892, William M’Lean’a
account at the Federal Bank was £25,314 in debit. So he


stepped across the road to the Commercial Bank, and
arranged with to draw a £25,000 cheque on that in-
stitution and pay it into the Federal for "balancing
purposes.” Next day he drew a little £25,000 cheque
on the Federal and pair) it into the Commercial, where-
upon Gyles Turner must have rejoiced to find how punc-
tual friend M’Lean was in putting his account straight.
A guileless Court will, it seems likely, send for Gyles
Turner to “explain” this transaction, as though it
couldn’t speak for itself.
• * * *
The Mutual Assurance Society of Victoria (like
the Mutual Life of Australasia) proposes, in its future
balance-sheets, to follow The Bulletin’s recent suggestion
by showing outstanding rents and interest, and rents and
interest accrued but not yet due, as two separate items.
The wisdom of this course is easily apparent. Provided
that an office does not follow the nefarious custom of
disguising overdue interest by adding it to the capital
sum of the mortgages as shown in the balance-sheet—
and the Colonial Mutual, Mutual of Australasia and
Mutual Of Victoria all distinctly announce that they have
never done this—the prudence of its investments may be
roughly gauged by these signs: (1) The amount of in-
terest realised—if this is abnormally high it suggests
recklessness, and if it is abnormally low it sometimes
means that large sums of interest have had to he written
off as irrecoverable, in which case it often happens that
the capital is also irrecoverable; (2) the amount of property
acquired by foreclosure ; and (3) the amount of rents and
interest “outstanding”—which term generally meansthat
a good part of the amount is in the first stage of putrefac-
tion. In the absence of a complete system of State audit
these are the only evidences on which the public can form
any kind of estimate, therefore every society should show
separately the amount of property acquired by foreclosure
as distinguished from that which was bought for
office purposes, and the interest and rent outstanding in
contradistinction to that which is merely accrued but not
yet due. The Mutual of Victoria already does this so far
as concerns the property-account with good results, and,
it is understood that the overdue interest item—which
is very small in any case—will also be sub-divided for the
future.

William Farmer, baronet or something like
that, and head of the great Sydney firm of Farmer & Co.,
returned to New South Wales, the other day, after an
absence of 20 years. The employes- 531 of them—held a
great pionio in his honor, and announced in the usual
fashion that he was a jolly good fellow, which, so far as
he was personally concerned, seemed quite a reasonable
statement. Tub Bulletin had somehow altogether for-
gotten that Fanner’s was another of those big Australian
firms whose profits mostly go to Hingl ind and help to
swell the annual £20,000,000, or so, drained out of this
beggared country, and it is sorry to he reminded of the
tact. Apparently there is only about one big firm out of
ten or more whose profits don’t go to some chronic
absentee or other in far-off Hingland. The only Joy
about the system is that, while every absentee is helping
to ruin the country which maintains him, he is also help-
ing to bring along the time when he will be ruined him-
self. But the time comes very slow'ly.
* * ♦ •
The Victorian Full Court has decided that poor,
down-trodden Matthew Davies is to get an unconditional
certificate of discharge, despite the decision of the Judge
in Insolvency to the contrary. Also, the Chief-Justice
went out of his way to sympathise with Matthew D., and
to point out the spotless purity of the man. “There
came a financial narrowing,” said the soulful Madden,
“the result being that the various companies in which
the insolvent’s funds were invested broke down, the mort-
gages became worthless, and he was stranded. The insol-
vent should not be held responsible for this state of
things.” The Pull Court was apparently quite oblivious
of the fact that the crushed Matthew didn’t exactly
invest his funds in the companies in question ; as a
rule he created the companies, and in some of them
he didn’t really invest anything, while in others
he only invested borrowed funds which he took
out of other companies, and in most instanees
the money which he invested in his companies
wasn’t a circumstance to the amounts which he got out
of them. The attempt to paint Matthew as a spotless
lamb who foolishly deposited his own solid private fortune
in unlucky companies, is a flue example of the way things
get turned upside down when the public has begun to
forget the real inwardness of the situation. So far,
nobody has protested against Madden’s exposition of the
case, a id it would seem as if people were almost ready to
believe that Matthew Davies actually put his own funds,
instead of other people’s, into his ghastly array of burst-
up banks.
• * • •
Premier Turner involves himself in anomalies
when he adopts the municipal valuations as his basis.
The assessment of unimproved values as 50 per cent, of
the whole capital value may average up all right, hut it
will deal out endless individual injustices. The direction
of least resistance is valuation by the owner, with the
governmental option of purchase. The admission of
municipal valuations, without any cheeky or safeguard,
means the admission of log-rolling and evasion and abuse.

The Vic. banks have almost reached their
meanest depth. Last week they got into court as
anomalous debtor-creditors in the estate of the late
William Pearson, M.L.C. The following items of the will
were submitted to the courts :
Deposit receipt, City of Melb. Bank, Limited, for £9OO,
due 16th June, 1898, at 4 per cent., £675.
Ditto, due 16th June, 1899, £675.
Ditto, due 16th June, 1900, £675.
Ditto, due 16th June, 1901, £675.
Ditto, due 16th June, 1903, £675.
1010 shares Colonial Bank, no market value.
2824 shares City of Melb. Bank, no market value,
1872 shares Commercial Bank, no market value.
1728 shares Bank of Victoria, no market value.
The decision of the Full Court was that probate need only
he paid on the market value of the deposit-receipts.
The shares were not to be rated assets, but liabilities to
the amount of the calls.

« * * *
The Turner Ministry’s new bill dealing with the
management of companies—which provides, among other
things, that it shall be criminal for directors to declare
divs. except out of realised profits, or to lend the share-
holders’ money to themselves, or to do several other
glaring things—roused Patterson to indignation. “The
end of it all will he,” he bellowed, “that we will all be
in gaol except Hancock.” Which seems to imply that
Hancock is the only honest man left in the Vic. Legisla-
ture, or else he is to escape on a technical point.

The N.S.W. Stamp Duties officers could collect
a lot of money from those companies—other than mining
co.’s—who put their scrip transfers through without the
necessary stamp-duty. Mining co.’s are the only ones
legally exempt, hut it is well-known that countless other
co.’s, either wilfully or ignorantly, consider themselves
exempt also. If Govt, had power to examine the scrip-
books of these oo.’s, a golden harvest would be reaped by
the State—from people who could well afford it.

Mr. P. P. Samuel, of Mount Hoaxley celebrity,
swore, in N.S.W. Bankruptcy Court, that a £12.000 hotel
of his in George-street, Sydney, had lain unprofitahly on
his hands for nearly five years, during which he had been
unable to get even ground-rent for it; while, for the last
three years, he had had to put in a manager to keep it
open. Little snags such as that are more effectively anti-
liqnor-seliing than all the commination-services of ail the
prohibitionists in the world.
* ♦ ♦ •
S.A. Govt. Credit Fonder Bill, now before the
Assembly, is mainly based on the French system. Govt,
takes over the Savings Bank and issues notes of a total
value not exceeding £750,000. Producers will he able to
secure long loans at lower Interest than is exacted by
private usurers. Of course, the great question is—Can
five trustees be obtained who will administer such a
system without being corrupted by political influence?
The reconstructed St. James’ Building Society
(Melb.) announces that its depositors, who are now fixed
for three years, will have to he extended to 10 years, and,
in the meantime, they must be content with 3' per shent.
Interest instead of 5. Also, at the recent meeting, sundry
birds of evil omen arose and said that the society’s
leaseholds, valued in the balance-sheet at £113,000, were
only worth £13,000, at the most. Which was possibly
true. It is a mighty good leasehold that is worth the
price of a drink just now.

The Thomas Bent Land Co. (Melbourne) is in a
very had way, and proposes to wind up. Twelve years
ago, it seems, Thos. Bent, finding he had a lot of property
on hand, floated himself into a co v and the above is the
final result. Thomas, being round and fat, floated
well for some time, but now he is old and heavy and
soaked with the waters of affliction, and is about to sink.

Broken Hill Block 10’s half-yearly report is
depressing. Nett result of six months’ work, a cash loss
of £9802, though much of this went to deveiope the mine.
605 tons ore were sold for t'5622, and 1552 ions of sul-
phides were realised and sold at 14s. 3d. per ton. The
mine has about 40,000 tons of sulphides on hand, and 625
tons from this lot were treated for 18s. lOd. per ton. The
co.’s future depends upon whether these sulphides can
be more economically treated—of which at present there
are but vague hopes. The co. has still £30,774 to credit,
and experiments are proceeding.

The “big mine,” Broken Hill, is, comparatively,
very short of lead again, and is fossicking in all directions
for it. There are hopes of getting a good body of it to-
wards Block 14 boundary. The Central and South people
quite expect to find a buyer for their lead (of which they
both have large quantities) in the Prop. Co. before twelve
