* * » •
White Cliffs (N.S.W.) opal-field is still under a
cloud. Many men, tributors mostly, whose parcels were
sent to Europe months and months ago, are hanging
about and would he very glad to sell the right to their
share of the returns for 50 per cent, of what the gems
were valued at when they left. Generally, also, the tribu-
ting conditions are unsatisfactory.
* * * *
Litigation as to the ownership of the valuable
Homeward Bound (Yalwal, N.S.W,) gold-mine is still
proceeding. No less than seven barristers appeared in
an appeal last week. The Bar can be backed to duffer-
out any reef under Heaven.
* * • •
The Caledonian (Shoalhaven) tributors, who gob
something like 800 oz. gold out of 34cwt. stone, and who
are still bagging stone in the same claim, are understood
to have only two months of their tribute to run. What is
hell? Hell, dear brethren, is having a rioh tribute with
only two months to run.

The exploratory crosscut from the 1330 ft. level
of the Extended (8.H.) shaft, having been abandoned by
the associated companies, has been taken over by the
Central, under whose workings the crosscut was being
driven. The work is being continued (laterally) by means
of a diamond drill.

Mining, writes a Bulletin correspondent, has
lately got a fresh impetus in the Shoalhaven (N.S.W.) dis-
trict in the vicinity of Barber’s Creek, many who were
working thereabouts, 20 to 30 years ago, having returned
to re-prospect their old claims, but, owing to landslips,
which, in some oases, have huri'd shafts, there is much
difficulty in finding the land-marks.
Henry Nolan and party are hard at work on a
1J mile race. This is a plucky enterprise by six men who
deserve to be rewarded for their large amount of dead
work. In one place the race had to he cut, for 6 or 7
chains, lift, deep through a hill, and about a mile further
on they are tunnelling for 10 or 12 oheine, through herd
shale and bine slate rook. They expect to finish the race
by Christinas. Their lease of 10 acres is on private pro-
perty. Most of the gold in the vicinity of Barber’s Creek
is very fine.

Bayley’s 15s. What a slump ! Anyone who
watched the yields, however, would have expected some-
thing to happen. The weekly yield is 700 oz. At first the
crushing was said to be about 40 tons. Then it ostensibly
mounted to 50, 60, and the last crushing was given as
700 oz. from 120 tons. The lower level stone is apparently
not up to much, and the stone in the upper levels is pro-
bably giving out. But you can only speculate about
Bayley’s. It is a rather close corporation.

The Wealth of Nations has struck the reef at
75ft., but the holders permit no inspection, and will give
no information except that “ the development is satisfac-
tory.” Having regard to previous facts, The Bulletin
surmises that the reef has been found to he composed of
24-carat gold cast into ingots and studded with ready-
cut brilliants and rubies. Reticence is therefore necessary.

W.A. Government, which, by the way, is,
more or less, personally interested in Coolgardie, cleans
up the Londonderry-hloek-jnmps litigation by the beauti-
fully simple process of passing a retrospective act validat-
ing the challenged regulations. The Ministry had the in-

the Courts remain exempt from the operations of the Act

However, Westralia isn’t civilised, bub is per-
meated by the squalor of Lagtime. Keynote : Election-
day at Perth—where one polling-booth suffices for the
whole country-side. Premier Forrest: " I say, Blank,
here’s a pretty go! Some beast has dumped down in
front of the booth a whole cart-load of offal. A lot of our
men won’t come near the place, hut old Mother is
dragging those blessed Irish voters across it by the
dozen.” You ean’t expect statesmen so environed to do
as Gladstone or Salisbury would do.

Out West they have a (gold) mining manager,
formerly in charge of a Barrier silver-mine, whose know-
ledge of ores is something wonderful. Recently a > isitor
asked him if, in his silver-mining experience, he had ever
come across a lode of ambergris. “ Oh, yes,” he replied,
“ the adjoining claim next to ours at the Pinnacles had a
big lode of it, and it assayed darned well for silver.” He
also tells visitors, who cannot see gold in his ore paddock,
that there is plenty gold in the stone, “but it’s oxidised.”
• • * •
At Day Dawn, Westralia, The Bulletin hears,
there is ai prospector who has been working on a small
reef for the last five months. He has sunk a shaft Soft.,
also driven 12ft., and has never had even one bucket-full
of material hauled or filled for him, but has done the
whole of it himself. He would fill a bucket and then
climb to the top and haul it up, and so on bucket after
bucket from first to last. Pitiable to relate, he has so far ,
got for all his labor only half-an-ounco of gold. May he
strike a patch!

Twenty W.A. mining companies floated in
London, recently, with a nominal capital of £1,722,000,
have paid £1,289,000 to vendors, while £433,000—a good
deal less than one-fourth—goes to develop the properties.
And even of that £433,000 probably not much more than
the odd thousands will ever actually reach the mines.

It is the vogue with Sydney softgoodsmen to
insist that every on engagement, shall sign an
agreement covenanting himself as liable to bo discharged
at a moment’s notice. ” Your services are no longer
required ” thus capsizes very frequently the apparently
placid security of a weekly billet. The employers pay
wages ny the week, and, when choosing to retrench a man
without a week’s notice, merely pay him a day’s wages.

• • • *
Editor Bulletin,—The reasons why the price of
antimony varies so in the Australian and London mar-
kets .... „
1. The antimony of commerce is “star antimony', a
product of crude antimony mixed with oxide of antimony,
tin clippings, and miscellaneous trifles ; but as the star
market is controlled by a “ ring,” which carefully leaves
a 30 per cent, margin, they are an important factor.
2. Crude antimony being only 70 per cent, metal (bal-
ance pure sulphur), and the metal easily oxidizing
(vaporising), it takes about 2 l-sth tons of metallic crude
to provide one ton of star.
3. Every smelting means loss of metal.
4. Three tons of 50 per cent, sulphide ore give roughly
one ton of crude antimony.
That, summed up, means that in one ton of star metal
there are 2j of crude or j\ tons of 50 per cent. ore. This,
mark you, without allowing any profit on smelting other
than the product of oxide obtained from the flues—the
same oxide being valueless here and mighty hard to dis-
pose of on the London market.—Sardine.
v « * • •
Christmas, of “ Christmas’s Patent Gold Ex-
tractor,” which is now under trial, is a Broken Hill
haker, who claims to deal effectively with fine-gold
stone and auriferous antimony. His principle is that
lead is heavier than antimony, and lead and gold have
affinity. Melt your antimony ore; throw in a little
lead and let it cool, when from the bottom of the cake
the lead, containing the gold, can be easily knocked
off the antimony, and the gold then got out of the
lead. Christmas-who tried the Pambula stone, hut
owns to a failure —has now taken a Vic. auriferous
antimony mine on tribute.
• * • •
An example of luck. Two miners were going
down the New Pinafore mine (Tas.), the other day, when
something went wrong and they dropped 170 ft., 1300 ft.
of rope falling on top of them. They weren’t hurt. When
your nuzzle is in you can drop about a quarter of a mile
with impunity, and when it is out you can’t step on the
smallest kind of a tack without setting up blood-poisoning.
And at Bendigo, two miners fell in a cage 1800 ft., and one
was not killed.

A Northern constable, who was a devout
Methodist, was in the habit of taking his prisoners
out of the cells on the Sabbath and marching
them off to the sanctuary. He tried the experi-
ment at his new district of Blackall and was, con-
sequently, relieved of his duties.
Cable : “ The workpeople of Russia have
deserted the factories and shops to join in the
requiem Masses to Alexander III.” By order of
the police ? Once, when the Governor of Kieff
died, the police ordered all the business places to
close in token of mourning till after the funeral.
There are not many really spontaneous demon-
strations in Russia.
Vic. Agricultural Department has sold a
shipment of rabbits in London at 30s. per dozen,
and hares at 725. per dozen ; while only 4s. and 6s.
respectively could be got in Melbourne. The total
cost of shipping, &c., was 7d. per rabbit or hare,
so there was a profit of Is. lid. each on rabbits
and ss. sd. each on hares. Large supplies are now
to go forward regularly, one London firm taking
4800 rabbits a week to begin with.
Sydney Telegraph is particularly amazing
when it gets on the subject of foreign politics. In
a recent leading article it announces that
“ Sweden and Norway thus both threatened (by
Russia) and equally prizing their independence . .
for purposes of mutual defence, decided to unite
their forces. . . The union had its origin in the
fear of Russian aggression.” The customary
historical account is that Norway—which hadn’t
possessed any shadow of independence for nearly
two centuries —was taken from Denmark and
given to Sweden by the Great Powers, Russia in-
cluded, as the price of Bernadotte’s assistance
against Napoleon, and that Sweden had to man
down Norway by force of arms before she could
enter into possession. But the Telegraph's
history, like its Freetrade statistics, resembles no
other history upon the face of this earth.

“ IMITATION
is the
SINCEREST FLATTERY.”
The articles most imitated are
CLEMENTS TONIC and
FLETCHER’S PILLS.
WHY?
BECAUSE they have the best reputation and
are most in demand by the public, consequently,
every dishonest trader tries to trade on their fame
and renown.
It is really wonderful that the Australian people
are so slow to see the truth as regards the un-
scrupulous quacks and charlatans, who prey on
the credulity of the Australian public. Scarcely a
week ever passes but what the press exposes the
barefaced and shameful practices of some of these
harpies, yet in face of all they seem to thrive, and
the paper which hounds them down most thrives
fattest on their advertisements and cracks cham-
pagne over each balance-sheet. Clements Tonic
and Fletcher’s Pills have never yet stooped to
deception and misrepresentation. They are
scientific therapeutical agents, and evidence as to
their value can be found in every district, and
such being the case it is unnecessary to further
extol their virtues.

Speaker Berry’s pretty and charming
daughters are a not inconsiderable factor in the
old Radical chief’s popularity. They have, per-
haps unconsciously, won him the friendship of
many young Conservatives who had no love for the
veteran’s political principles.
Why the jury should have returned a
verdict of suicide in the cases of both the brothers
Munk, found shot dead in their room at the
Oxford Hotel, Sydney, last week, is a puzzle. On
the face of the evidence there was nothing what-
ever to warrant such a conclusion; it is quite
probable the shooting was done, and the letters
written, by one hand alone.
Miss Katharine Hardy,
now playing with Maggie
Moore’s “Little Jack Shep-
pard” at Melb. Royal, is a
young lady blessed with a
pretty face, graceful figure,
and pure soprano voice. Miss
Hardy was born in England
and went to Maoriland a few
years back, where she soon
built up a reputation as a con-
cert-singer. In a representa-
tion of Mozart’s "Marriage
of Figaro ” at the Mozart
Centenary Festival of ’92 she
attracted the attention of Mel-
bournians, and since then she
has steadily worked her way
ahead. She played success-
fully in the brief season of
“The Adventuress” at Melb.
Bijou, and took part in
“Skipped by the Light of
the Moon,” and other produc-
tions. Miss Hardy scored as
leading lady with Walter
Bentley in his M.L. tour of
’92, and while with Manning’s
Opera Co. this year in the
Gulden West was warmly re-
ceived at Perth ; after the
opera season she played in a
couple of pieces with Jennie
Lee. Within the last few
weeks she has appeared at
Melb. Exhibition concerts and
made a distinct hit by her
genuinely artistic singing.
Miss Hardy’s present rdle at
the Royal does not give much
scope to her abilities, but what
she does is done well. She has
been unfortunate in the past
in being connected with some
badly-paying ventures, but it
is hoped that the Mascotte of
good luck which Maggie Moore
possesses will come within the
reach of the lady now support-
ing her. To the ad vantages of
superior personal charms Mias
Hardy adds refinement, intelli-
igence, and excellent conversa-
tional powers. With proper
opportunities, a bright operatic
career awaits her.

tbe Hebrews.
When J. F, Hogan, of “the Australian
party in the House of Commons,” was on Melb.
Argus his fellow pressmen nicknamed him “The
Virgin.” He invariably went about with a book
under his arm. Editor Haddon used to say that
Hogan was the most-finished paragraphist he had
ever met.
Dear Bulletin, —Have any of the pen-
ladies who scatter “ queenly ” so freely about their
Melbourne Cup lucubrations ever seen a queen ?
I’ve only seen four—all podgy, andoneof ’em blub-
bery; none of ’em tall, and three of ’em stumpy ;
none of ’em handsome, and two of ’em ugly ; not
one of them, in short, suggestive of anything beyond
a well-fed woman of extremely limited intelli-
gence.— Heretic).
UNION VERSUS BLACKLEG: THE TROUBLES OF THE
POOH SHEEP .
One of the best things got off in connec-
tion with the Speight v. Syme case was a couplet
on Herbert Low, erstwhile Age reporter, witness
against Syme, and well-known verse-writer ;
Low, the poor injured, whose untutored rhyme
Sees God in Speight and Beelzebub in Syme I
Max O’Rell, after remarking that in Aus-
tralia drink is the panacea against dullness,
relates how, when he arrived at a certain Vic.
town where he proposed to lecture, he found the
mayor, town-clerk, principal bank-manager, pro-
prietor of the leading hotel, and the representative
of the district in Parliament—all drunk. No
wonder. They had heard of his coming and had
taken the panacea in advance.
Mrs. Besant will probably be writing an
article for one of the English reviews on some
political and social impressions of Australasia,
and especially of Maoriland, where she was struck,
singularly enough, with the extreme forwardness
of the people in political matters, combined with
their extraordinary backwardness in general cul-
ture and scientific knowledge, as compared with
the inhabitants of the large Australian cities.
The late Dean Macartney, of Melbourne,
would never go through Fitzroy Gardena, though
he lived near them, and the way through was his
nearest route to the city—because he looked upon
the statuary as morally injurious. The morals of
an old gentleman of 90 don’t matter much, but he
thought the question important, and he always
impressed upon his women-folk and servants that
they were not to go through either for the same
reason.
Justice Hodges, who condemned from the
Bench Purves’ insinuation regarding surgeon
O’Hara’s thousand-guinea job, was once O’Hara’s
“boss.” When Mr. O’Hara came to Australia
first he was advised by his cousins, the Maddens,
to go to the law, and he became a pupil in Hodges’
chambers. O’Hara, however, found he had no
taste for law, and deserted it for surgery. Now,
if he had only stuck to the Bar and appeared in
Court with J.L.P. on the other side !
Attorney-General Isaac A. Isaacs, and his
brother John A. Isaacs, both members of Vic.
Parliament, are exactly alike, and t’other can
scarcely be distinguished from which. “I say,
which of you two is the other ? ” asked ex-Trea-
surer Carter, the other day. “lam the other,”
answered brother John. The father of these per-
tinacious young men addresses the elder as “Mr.
Isaacs,” and the younger as “Jack.” The dis-
tinction marks a wide difference in ability.
Is any living man in the front rank a teeto-
taller (except Edison, who is never without a
cigar in his mouth)? Balfour, Archbishop Ben-
son, Bismarck, Casimir-Perier, Caprivi, Cleveland,
Chamberlain, Crispi, Dufferin, Emperor Wilhelm,
Gladstone, Irving, Kipling, Millais, the Pope,
Rosebery, Salisbury, Sardou, Swinburne, Verdi,
Zola—all drink wine if not whisky. There is no
teetotal Australian Premier, and no one who is
likely to be an Australian Premier is a teetotaller.
G. A, Oruickshank, M.L. A., is one of the
few consistent N.S.W. moneyed men. He is said
to be almost as wealthy as Sydney Burdekin, but
invests only on station and country securities, and
then only on personal inspection. He prides him-
self that he can estimate to within a fiver the
value of any security, no matter how extensive,
and, what is more important still, has never yet
had to take over any property on which he has
made an advance. His maximum rate is 6 per
cent., and he avers if a mortgagor offers a higher
rate that there is something “cronk,” and he
would not touch it, even if it were backed by a
dozen M.’sP. !

It is related of a certain N.S.W. railway
magnate that, returning home one night in
the tram, he noticed that the windows dividing
the smoking carriage were covered with dust.
He said sharply to the conductor, “You don’t
keep your carriages very clean on this suburb!”
The guard, somewhat put out after a long day’s
work, and not recognising the magnate, answered
sharply, “ Mind your own business.” When
the magnate and his friends got out, those who
remained in the carriage were chuckling every
time the guard passed. At last this aroused his
attention, and he asked what they were laughing
at. They pointed to the window, on the pane of
which the magnate had traced with his finger, in
the dust, “Blank, commissioner for railways.”
Truly a superfine sense of humor !
Dick Seddon, the six-foot M.L. Premier,
was a most uncouth customer when first sent to
Parliament by the Knrnara diggers. In his first
speech he wrestled with the word “schedule,”
which he dubbed “skeddle,” The word has
stuck, and has been adopted by many members as
the more convenient form of pronunciation. Dick
detested the late Vincent Pyke. Once in the
small hours during a stonewall debate, when
members were lying about in blankets on the
benches, more or less drunk and snarly, little
podgy Vincent was helping his side by a general
discourse. Suddenly Seddon electrified the
drunken and half -awake crowd by shouting out in
a deep bass, “You’re a -liar!” The Speaker’s
attention was drawn to this, but he said he didn’t
hear it, and it suddenly dawned on everybody
that it was best to let the matter drop. Pyke
was boiling and purple. He went out into the
lobby, got a big stick and lay in wait for his
elephantine antagonist; but Seddon, being warned,
stayed in the house, and friends after a while
managed to get Pyke away to bed.


ADVICE TO TOURISTS.
The Briton enjoys his pipe at home. When
travelling through France he sticks to it more
faithfully than ever, for he hates cigarettes. In
Germany he thinks a cigar now and then will do
him no harm. In Switzerland his pipe seems,
somehow or other, to drop completely into
oblivion, and wherever you meet him there you
will find him with a look of contentment beaming
on his face, and a Frossard’s Cavour in his mouth.
These cigars have now been brought out to Aus-
tralia, and can be had at all tobacconists at the
price of only one shilling for a packet of eight.
They are mild and aromatic, and better value has
never yet been given.

POLITICAL POINTS
Throttled liberty gasps out riots.
The R.C. representation in Vic. Assembly
increased from 7 to 17 at the general elections.
Ex-Premier Patterson has the gall to tell
the Vic. Assembly that a tax on land values is an
“unheard-of ” proposal.
Premier Kingston, having failed to induce
Vic. Premier Turner to settle the boundary ques-
tion, will shortly force the dispute into the law-
courts.
The time has long come for a democratic
daily in Melbourne, and the opening for a live
evening paper is surely a sufficiently tempting
chance.
It is whispered in the innermost temples
of Victorian politics that the alternative to the
acceptance of Turner’s taxation proposals is
Repudiation.
“Wanted—an invention to prevent a mort-
gagee adding the amount of any possible land-tax
to the mortgagor’s interest when the latter wants
a renewal.” This is where a State Land Bank
would come in.
In a Police-court case recently heard in a
little Maori land town, one of the J.sP. on the
Bench was the father of the plaintiff’s lawyer and
the other was his father-in-law. N.B.—The
plaintiff won.
Berry-Service, Griffith-M ‘ll wraith, Parkes-
Robertson : why should a Parkes-Dibbs coalition
be considered impossible in Australian politics?
That such a coalition would be immoral doesn’t
affect the matter.
The field-night given by N.S.W. Assem-
bly to Australian Federation—a reckless waste of
time —was merely an expensive sop to the uncurb-
able vanity to H. Parkes. Federation is once
more “ in the hair.”
Counting pairs, the first test division, that
approximately defined the state of parties for and
against the Reid Government in N.S.W., took
place last week, the figures being 69 and 27
respectively. Only 28 members were absent.
“Land,” says the Argus, “is the raw
material of all our exporting industries. There-
fore, land should be taxed as little as the neces-
sities of the State will allow.” No. Therefore,
it should be taxed so heavily as to make the use
of the material by the industrious absolutely im-
perative.
Q. Govt, now holds
aloof from Federation.
Federation means united
action to exclude cheap
labor, and Boodle-
wraith and its banks are
flourishing on cheap
labor. Nelson and Co. are
content that the cause of
Democracy and of Aus-
tralia should perish so
long as their greed is
glutted.
Few things have
made the Sydney
Foreign-trade press feel
worse of late than
MMillan’s speech on the
financial statement. In
it he pointed out that
the DibbsGovt. deserved
well of the country for
the immense reductions
which it made in the
expenditure ; that the
practice alleged by Pre-
mier Reid of cutting
down the estimates of
expenditure to make a
bogus surplus, and then
demanding large sums on
the supplementary esti-
mates to make things
square, was mostly flam,
said supplementary esti-
mates last year being
only £300,000 ; and that
the promised limiting of
the unauthorised expen-
diture to .£50,000 was
foolish and impracticable.
All which, and especially
the statement that the
late Protectionist Govt,
had “saved the country
in its hour of peril,” has
made the Foreign trade
press gnash its best Sun-
day teeth.
The tone of H. Parkes’s latest federation
speech :
And, Believing myself a loyal subject of the greatest
Sovereign that ever lived—my Queen— I cannot but
be anxious about the future of the Empire of which we
form a part.
Attorney-General Simpson and Premier
Reid are the only opponents to Local Option with-
out compensation in present N.S.W. Cabinet, and
yet, under a professed system of government by
majority, the voices of these two will swamp the
influence of eight other Ministers.
The sanctified Professor Rentoul denies
“with a thousand noes ” that Christ came as a
social reformer. “If he had come as such,
whoops the affluent Vic. saint, “ His effort would
have perished impotent! ” Even the Lord ceases
to be Almighty pitted against “ proputty ” and
privilege.
S.A. Legislative Council passed Kingston’s
Progressive Tax Bill by 12 votes to 9, included in
the majority being the six labor members and
Russell and Bice who displaced confirmed
Tories. S.A. Council is in reality much more
democratic and representative than the unwieldy
Assembly.
A while ago it was W. J. Lyne who,
according to babbling rumor, stood barefooted
waiting to jump into N.S.W. Railway Commis-
sioner Eddy’s boots; now the same authority
gives the pose and yearning to Minister Young.
Something in the air of the Public Works Depart-
ment accounts for these noisy guesses.
Lawyers, who are members of the strongest
trades-union in the world, are the most strenuous
opponents of trades-unionism. They are, also,
almost the only people who are insured a minimum
wage by the laws of the country, and are —shall
we say therefore?— out-and-out in their protests
against the layman getting a minimum wage.
Monseigneur Couppd, R.C. Bishop of New
Britain, now visiting Sydney, is a plain-spoken
opponent of black-labor recruiting systems, and
attributes to the deportation of islanders most of
the native crime. He reckons that 70 to 75 out
of every 100 New Britishers recruited for New
Guinea die before their contract is completed.
Several of Parkes’s friends contend chat
bis late speeches in debate prove his intellect up
to the level of 15 years ago, and with the conten-
tion suggest his prime fitness for leadership as
soon as an opening can be made. So far as Parkes
himself is concerned he thinks that for alertness
and brightness he is an improvement on 30 years
ago.
N.S. W. Parliamentary Elections and Quali-
fications Committee should at once be made a
non-political tribunal. Look, for instance, at the
false position occupied by member Crick. At one
time, he sits in judgment on the case of his
deadly enemy, Black; at another, he helps to
decide the political fate of his friend and quondam
partner, Willis.
Varney Parkes, in throwing up his seat
on N.S.W. Public Works Committee, sacrifices
an average of £l2 12s. a week.. Some years ago
he resigned his seat in Parliament to devote him-
self, as he said, to money-making by his profes-
sion, and it appears that either he has been suc-
cessful, or the offences of the Works Committee
must smell rankly to him.
Certain Protectionist authorities vow that
if the Reid Government allows party animus to
influence it in discharging Protectionist pro-
teges from N.S.W. public service, Protectionists
will, when the wheel turns, behave similarly
towards Freetrade proteges. This would be a
natural exercise of lex talionis, but why should the
country submit to either party having proteges?

The endowment clauses of Premier Reid’s
Local Government Bill will, if passed in their
present state, stimulate instead of killing off the
Parliamentary chances of the local alderman.
The man whose qualification for a seat in the
House is that he will work to get a gaol built in
his district appears to be expressly provided for
in the bill, whereas he should have been once for
all exterminated.
All the Labor-members in S.A. Assembly-
voted against the granting of a license to sell in-
toxicating liquors on Lake Bonney (Govt.) Irriga-
tion Settlement unless at the request of the
majority of the adult residents. They were
beaten, however, the chief argument on the other
side being that, as the settlers were to grow grapes
and make wine, it would be inconsistent to pro-
hibit them from drinking it.
Cook and Son, the excursionists’ agents,
have been raking in over £lOO a month clear
profit out of the sale of Vic. railway tickets, a
concession granted them by Dickey Speight and
Dunk Gillies. Considering that the chief business
of the Railway Dept, is to sell tickets, for which
purpose it keeps an immense staff that spends a
good deal of its time reading American dime-
novels, the idea of subsidising a rival concern is
characteristic of Gillies. The present Govern-
ment has discharged Cook and Co., and the Rail
way Dept, is now expected to mind its own
business.
HONESTY REPULSED.
SHE: “ Don’t you think you had better allow me to take charge of that stray
fowl and advertise for its rightful owner, my boy?
SMALL BOY: “No yer don’t, lady. I know yer little game. Just go and buy
yer own ’at feathers, and not come yer confidence dodges on
’spectable persons. ”
Two nominee Upper Houses crushed
Lower House decisions in a similarly emphatic
way last week. Queensland Legislative Council,
by the balance of the House to two members,
killed the Lower House resolution in favor of an
increased Payment of Members, and N.S.W.
Legislative Council, also by all votes against two,
quashed the Assembly decisions in favor of the
Eight Hours clause in the Mining Bill.
A certain very democratic Southern poli-
tician is prepared to defend the theory that it is
against the national good that any Australian-
born politician should be allowed to power
who has ever visited England. Did Washington,
he asks, ever visit England ? Did Jefferson ? And
has not up to date almost every Australian poli-
tician who has ever visited England been hope-
le sly corrupted from a national-republican point
of view ?
Premier Reid’s income-tax exempts in-
comes derived from N.S.W. Govt, debentures,
'provided such debentures are not held by per-
sons resident in the province.” The absentee
debenture-holder is to get his interest intact,
while the ordinary “ colonial ” one gets his less
income-tax! Therefore, it comes to the same
tiling as offering a higher rate of interest to the
foreign investor than to the Australian one. This
is Protection turned upside down with a venge-
ance.
The fact that W. Wynne, of Sydney D.T.,
and D. Syme, of Melb. Age, were willing to take
up 20,000 shares of the defunct Melb. Standard,
to prevent its assimilation by the Herald, would
tend to prove that the greed of enterprise unites
strange comrades. Wynne is the outward
sign of foreign-trade in the N.S.W. press ; Syme
is the roaring dictator of protection in Victoria.
Was the proposed revivified Standard to have
grog-blossomed out as a “ freetectionprotrading ”
Janus ?
The S. M. Herald’s idea of fair play.
Haynes, M.L.A., in the recent N.S.W. Assembly
Federation debate, made a long speech against the
dead-and-buried Convention Bill. The Herald
compressed it into four lines in its report.
M’Millan replied as forcibly as he knew bow. The
Herald gave him nearly half-a-column of its
valuable space. It is not necessary to agree with
Haynes to see the monstrous injustice of this and
denounce it.
*Gov. Duff talked a pitiful lot of Imperialis-
tic bombast at the Melbourne Mayor’s banquet.
“ No—haw —statesman or candidate for Parlia-
mentary honors could—haw—now appeah before
the English people as an advocate of the—haw-
little England policy.” If Gov. Duff means by
this that no politician in England would stand up
and condemn the “expansion” plunder-and-
blood business, then he simply talked Ass. bray.
Has Gov. Duff never heard, for instance, of Henry
Labouchere ?
Premier Reid, out of sympathy for the
burdens placed upon the poor in N.S.W., is going
to take off his predecessor’s duties upon imported
silks, satins, laces, jewellery, precious stones,
works of art, pianos, &c., &0., and make up part
of the loss by levying a land-tax which comes
down with exactly the same weight upon the
miserable “ cocky ” with 100 acres and the auto-
crat with 100,000. True, the amount at issue
isn’t much, but, none the less, G. Ti. Reid should
stop snuffling about the burdens laid on the poor
man. Also, he should give up calling his parti-
cular land-tax an impost on Wealth, for it is
nothing of the kind.
M‘Millan will not, until he
is financially independent, accept office in any
N.S.W. Government, and considers his deter-
mination a virtuous one. Let us suppose, how-
ever, that an epidemic of such determinations
swept over the land. Where then would the State
be? Probably there are not more than a hundred
financially independent men within the boun-
daries of N.S. Wales, and of these less than ha f-
a-dozen would be returned, or deserve to be
returned, to Parliament at a general election, and
hardly one of them would be fit to be a IVXinister.
And Mr. M’Millan calls himself a patriot.

One of the chief objections to Premier
Reid’s N.S.W. budget non-proposals was that he
was staving off his promises and postponing his
declared plans of new taxation. Yet, when the
deliverer of his country rushed the Assembly at a
late-early hour in the night-morning with the
introduction in committee of his L an d and In-
come Tax Assessment Bill, there was a general
Opposition howl that his action was too
“previous.” Which provides another choking
satire on “ Government by Contradiction.”
The Foreign-trade S.M. Herald “ blows
cold ” on the tariff fetish it was wont to idolise :
No considerations of so-called principle, which may
mean nothing more than the pur-uit of a dream, should
be allowed to divert the attention of the Government
from the necessity of caution and care in the introduction
of extensive fiscal changes.
The very abandon of candour ! The secret main-
spring of the Herald is that no considerations of
principle, so-called or genuine, should be allowed
to influence anything anyhow, What price the
much-advertised Freetrade conscience?
We now stand at the parting of the ways.
This legal conspiracy to hoist Australian Judges
on to the English Privy Council Bench to hear
Australian appeals in London is an incident of
tremendous importance. Shall we, by sending
not representative members to England’s Parlia-
ment, but what would be more momentous still,
representative Judges to England’s Bench, post-
pone for half-a-century the coming of the inevit-
able Australian national-republican final Court of
Appeal? The present lawyers’ conspiracy involves
a big step towards Imperial Federation ; an Aus-
tralian final Court of Appeal means the Republic.
The Bulletin confidently leaves the issue to the
Democratic party in every Australian province,
Salomons, ex-Vice-President of N.S.W.
Executive Council, and ex-Chief Justice, backed
up by the whole force of the Crown Law Office,
appeared before N.S.W. Banco Court, last week,
to argue in support of an appeal, in the case
known as “Watt and Gilchrist and the Crown
Lands Act of ’B4 and ’B9 and the Court, after
listening to Salomons’ long speech, unanimously
dismissed the appeal, without calling upon the
other side for a word in rebuttal. Darley, C. J.,
said there was not a particle of ground on which
to rest the appeal; Windeyer, J., fully concurred
with that opinion, and Innes, J., remarked that
“really the case was too clear for argument.”
Yet there were Salomons and Canaway and
Rogers and Pike, in wig, and Ellis and Button
and three or four other solicitors, all down for
fees in the case, and the country must foot the
bill.

Mr. Justice Windeyer, in giving judgment in the divorce-
suit of Stratford v. Stratford, had occasion to refer to the manner
in which a witness had given his evidence. He said the man had
shuffled his feet about in the box in a manner which was quite
sufficient to convince anyone accustomed to weighing evidence
that he was not speaking tho truth. His Honor added that he
was not sure that the Irish system, under which
witnesses were placed upon a table in full view of
the Court, had not many advantages connected
with it.

At Kempsey (N.S.W.) police court, on
Nov. 13, before Sutherland, Bond and Clarke,
Js.P., Hill, J.P., was found not guilty of allowing
his horse to stray on the highway. Then, before
Hill, J.P , Sutherland, J.P. got a verdict in the
case of a workman who sued him for wages. Ap-
parently, the Kempsey justices—like most other
justices—are kept pretty busy finding verdicts in
each other’s case*. The spectacle of a J.P. de-
ciding a case in favor of another J.P., and then
getting down off the bench to appear as defendant
while the other defendant climbs up and promptly
hears his case, is (without necessarily imputing
dishonesty to any member of the Bench) one of
several hundred things which go to make J.P.-ism
a scandal to any civilised community.
# * #
Five J.sP., constituting the Magisterial
Bench at Bowral, N.S. W., lately petitioned the
Magisterial Bench at Picton, urging the latter to
use clemency towards a youth accused of larceny
and coining up for trial, the ground on which the
petition was based being that the youth’s parents
were respectable and had lived many years in
Bowral. The Picton Bench fined the lad 405.,
with an alternative of 14 days’ imprisonment.
« • •
An incident happened last week in Auck-
land (M.L.), which finished the story of the once-
notorious Probert will-case. Old Probert died
some three or four years ago, leaving a good-siz d
fortune and an aged, crippled and penniless
brother. He left the brother .£lOO and the
balance —over .£20,000 —to the Wesleyan Church.
The surviving Probert went round feebly for a
while on two dotty legs, trying to demonstrate to
the Wesleyan Church that it might, in common
decency, allow him, say, .£lOO per annum for life
out of the .£BOO or .£lO9O a year income realised
from the bequest, but the church—as trustee for
“ Gawd ” —declined to see it, and at last the
matter dropped. Last week, having got through
his legacy, the piralytio brother hanged himself,
which ends the story. According to all the rules
of poetic justice he should have done his final
asphyxiation in the Wesleyan church, but pro-
bably he was too weak to get so far, or possibly
le church was locked and he couldn’t get in.
Dear Bulle-
tin, —The reason
the revolver is so
much discredited
is that few users
thereof have had a
hundredth part of
the necessary prac-
tice. This arm, in
any case, never
shoots as accurately
as the single or
double (rifled) pis-
tol, and only a re-
volver of the best
make—in the hand
of a man who has
practised continu-
ally with that in-
dividual weapon—
will give shooting
good enough to
hang your life on.
Hence the danger-
ous absurdity of
arming with a tick-
lish and uncertain
weapon a thick-
fingered Hibernian
policeman, who
may never have
fired a dozen rounds
in his life.—M.

The Duke of Norfolk is a sort of lay Pope
in England. He tried to get another Saxon
appointed to Sydney when Archbishop Vaughan
died, but Pope Leo sent out Dr. Patrick Moran.
Then Melbourne was to have a coadjutor, and
Norfolk was at it again. He tried to have Lord
Archibald Douglas, brother of Florence Dixie and
of Queensberry, the boxing man, sent out, but
Archbishop Goold wouldn’t have him on any
account. Then Dr. Goold died unexpectedly, and
an assistant wasn’t wanted, and the “ Man from
Galway ” was exported to Melbourne.

The Akgus Brisbane correspondent wires :
Queensland Meat Export Co. has received the following
cable message : “Duke of Argyll's hind-quarters selling
at 3Jd. per lb. ; fore-quarters at 2Jd. Quality and con-
dition excellent.”
American heiresses have hitherto been free buyers
of British aristocracy (by the carcase), and The
Bulletin can only account for the low prices
alised in London for the primest portion of His
Grace’s anatomy by assuming that Argyll’s Ugly
red hair and freckles were not confined to his head
only, or that he had indulged too freely in
scratching-posts and thereby damaged his meat.
Anyhow, it’s satisfactory to know that even the
very choicest brand of “ long pig ” can be bought
in London at half the price per pound of guaran-
teed-genuine pork sausages.
# # •
The blacks of Westralia have “ foully
murdered ” a Christian constable for no con-
ceivable cause, except that he had 17 natives on
the chain and was driving them off to prison.
Perhaps the poor ignorant beasts imagined a
personal grievance against the foreign person who
was enforcing the principles of law’norder. Any-
how, the duty of the Westralian Government is
clear. The local tribes must be exterminated
forthwith. The process of removing them to the
haunts of civilisation in chained batches might
presently result in the loss of another valuable
policeman. Killing’s the only cure for native in-
subordination.
• * •
Fifteen murders were committed in Vic-
toria during the first ten months of 1894. The
universal poverty and wretchedness which fol-
lowed the bursting of the boom, and the outbreak
of hysterical religion which came on top of the
financial disasters, are the only apparent causes
of this unusual outbreak of violence. So, thfe
ghosts of the dead may be fairly distributed
among certain distinguish! d boornsters and bank-
managers, many of whom have lately been white-
washed ; a spectre and a-half may also be allowed
to roost on the doorstep of Muokneill & Co. The
balance may be set down to drink, jealousy, and
original sin, as usual.

Apparently, it was the New Woman who
largely contributed to the rapid breaking-up of
the true-mateship ideal of Rue Australia. The
Moabitish leaven is the omnipresent ferment in
every Israelitish pilgrimage. And Rebecca is
always jealous of the Midianitish woman. Moses
Lane being unable to miraculously suppress the
natural fires of Rue Australia, and not having to
hand an enthusiastic Phinehas armed with a
righteous javelin, the community simply fell to
pieces. The story is as old as trouble. Even the
prize-fighter Samson was knocked out by a woman.
It is the male destiny.

Awhile ago a Chinaman, suspected of being
leprous, was quarantined at Port Augusta (S. A.)
hospital, but a medical expert subsequently pro-
nounced the disease to be of another character,
and John, since August last, has been hawking
vegetables around Adelaide A few days ago,
however, he was again examined, and grave doubts
arise as to whether it is not leprosy after all. So
far, this last feature of the case has been kept out
of the daily press.

Dear Bulletin, —“ Max O’Rell ” only
follows stereotyped Hinglish spleen (unwisely
encouraged, it is true, by loose Australian scrib-
blers who aim only to achieve a passing wheeze)
in vividly accentuating “ larrikinism ” as a vicious
trait indigenous only to the Australian character.
The approach of the Christmas season reminds
me of the vogues that were prevalent in my
juvenile days, at this time of the year, among
that much-belauded sample of cub-humanity—the
English schoolboy. How many Bulletin readers
will remember the pranks they played as school-
boys on the three Sundays preceding the half-
yearly “breaking-up.” The first Sundiv was
“ Churchwood Sunday.” On that 1 iy, •• \• iy buy
was pledged, under penalty of being kicked
and cuffed and pinched mercilessly by every
other boy during the interval between church
and dinner, to bring a\yay with him a
piece of timber out of the church gallery.
The lad who could wrench, put a staircase-ban-
nister and march off with it inside a leg of his
trousers was a hero ! What was this? ■ Larrikm-
ism? No feah —British playfulness,dontcherknow!
The next Sunday was “Gob Sunday,” when every
boy (and they numbered about 90) w|§' -required
to spit in as nearly exactly the Same spot as every
other boy on the last step of the church-gallery
staircase. Was this larrikinism? Oh, dear no—
British humah, dontcherknow ! The last Sunday
was “ Runaway Sunday the routine being that,
when the boys were ordered to >“ form fours ” to
march back to school, they and all bolted.
Not larrikinism, deah boy—only British playful-
ness, dontcherknow, meah/ animal spiwits.—
Yours, Dipso.

Prior to Dr. Kennion’s departure from
Adelaide to take the see of Bath and Wells, the
local clergy of all denominations joined together
to mumble eulogies concerning his S.A. career.
His first cabled utterance in England is a thanks-

long wash of Australasian seas.

JcS.W. “Native Flora Protection Bill,”
prohibiting wild-flower shows and selling, and
punishing destruction of plants, is right in essence,
but goes a little too far. It should be made clear
that merely to pluck flowers on unenclosed land,
if not for sale, is not illegal; otherwise no pic-
nicker will be safe.

Australian “loyalty” again. Behind the
dais occupied by Gov. Gormanston at the opening
of Tas. Exhibition, was hung a screen decorated
with unionjacks and ensigns and royal standards
and similardrapery. Unluckily, this interfered with
the view of the choristers, who protested that they
hadn’t dressed themselves nicely in white uni-
forms with blue and red sashes in order to be
hidden from public approval by any flags
whatever. The directory at first refused to
remove tne screen, and the choir was just
about to lay violent hands on it when work-
men were sent to pull down the glorious relics
of the battle and the breeze. Then the choir
smoothed down its whiteuniforms and straightened
up its red and blue sashes, and smiled at the
audience, and the audience applauded and smiled
back-;* and-when. Gov, Gormanston did the open*
ing a few minutes later he had to knock out tin
most effective period in the speech he had learnt,
because he couldn’t strike an attitude and point to
the trophy of flags. But the choir sang with
much feeling a cantata commencing
Here no din of war affrights us ;
Here no sounds of strife resound ;
and the next time an Australian Exhibition
directory wants to display the insignia of Austra-
lian “ loyalty ”it will take care to hang them up
on the rafters out of the way.

The girls who dispense tea and tarts at a
large Sydney refreshment-room state that it is nob
uncommon for “lady” customers to steal the
natty milk-jugs and sugar-basins, and frequently
the miniature teapots. In the absence of a bag an
umbrella is the “ trap ” used.
“The worst and cleverest prisoners are
the first to make friends with the gaol-chaplain
and get into his good graces, and work on the
good feelings of those they meet to deceive
them,” says Judge Backhouse (N.S.
Wales). Yes, and it is equally
true that the least criminal
prisoner, the most manly con-
vict, the honest blunderer, or
the least hypocritical offender
with at all events the courage
of defiance, is the man who chafes
uncontrollably under restraint, who makes
breaches in gaol-regulations, and incautiously
vents “disrespectful” language at some
warder who is far and away beneath even
the prison grade of manhood, and becomes
the “ incorrigible ” who gets flogged and
has every redeeming point cut out of him
piecemeal by the murderous flagellator, the
bowelless instrument of a bloody system.
The Redfern accident has recalled to many
Melbournians a narrow escape from a similar dis-
aster which occurred at Eliuders-street station on
the7th March, ’B7. One train was timed to leave
as soon as another, the packed 9 o’clock Brighton
express, arrived. It happened that the express
was a few minutes late, and the driver of the
ordinary train, by mere mechanical force of habit,
moved out at his usual time. He had just got his
train across the track of the express as the latter
approached. In five seconds a frightful collision
would have occurred, but luckily the signalman
saw the danger, and, rushing through the window
of his box, he stood between the approaching
trains, red flag in hand, and just saved the im-
pact. Commissioners Speight and Greene, En-
gineer of Existing Lines Lunt, Assistant Traffic
Manager Francis, and Exhibition Secretary
Lavater were in the front van, and, with their
hearts in their mouths, saw the whole thing. A
very secret enquiry was held, with the result that
the driver of the ordinary train was heavily
punished, while the signalman, whose act had
saved a great loss of life and at least .£50,000, was
awarded .£5. About a fortnight after the Melb.
Herald scented out and published all the facts,
the other papers followed suit, and that was the
first hint the passengers got of what they had
escaped.
“ Z.” ; Apropos the statement of N.S.W.
Rail way Dept, that it isimpossihlefor the interlock-
ing gear to get out of order. 3orne four years ago,
when driver Bertram was killed at Cleveland-st.
tunnel, the Dept, then admitted that the fatality
was caused by faulty interlocking apparatus, and
paid Bertram’s mother something like .£6OO as
compensation. On that occasion the deceased
was taking a goods train to Darling Harbor when
an engine proceeding to Eveleigh ran into him.
Both drivers had their correct signals, but owing
to the interlocking gear failing to act the goods
train and engine collided, causing the instant
death of Bertram and extensive damage to rolling
stock. Also, at the Departmental enquiry on the
recent Redfern smash, the signalman stated that
about a quarter to nine he drank a cup of water,
and from that time till after the accident he felt
as if he was in a kind of haze and everything
seemed a blank to him. These facts were not
mentioned at the coroner’s inquest,

There is to be a vitriolic contest for
Adelaide mayoralty this year between Willcox,
present Mayor, and Aldermen Skethaway and
Tucker. The latter will probably be elected, as
he will spend much money, but his chances have
been much damaged by a public declaration of
Grainger, M.P., that Tucker was his greatest
friend.
# * #
Miller, M.P., proposes, in his new Bill for
the destruction of N.S.W. legal nepotism, to pass
a clause making a son-in-law a “ blood relation.”
This is the toughest job the present Parliament
has had before it, and is even beyond the region
of things that Reid—that wholesale promiser—
might be expected to take in hand.

ANY MAN TO ANY WIFE.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
This day waning—white is the stone that marks
it —
Gave me once thy heart to be mine, beloved,
Mine by love’s constraint that will hold us hence-
forth
One, till the night fall.
Lift to mine true eyes, and upon my bosom
Leaning all that burthen of tender beauty,
Bid thy heart speak once in its own clear language
Softly, to my heart.
Lurks no cloud, no Shadow in love’s bright
heaven ?
Threats no blighting wind blowing chill from
southward,
Love’s warm roses? Heart of my heart, speak
truly:
Am I not near thee ?
Me from old time sway’d with a charm all-
powerful,
Beauty; fear’st thou, sweet, lest the swift years
rob thee,
With that youthful grace, of the man’s strong
passion
Now at thy white feet ?
Somewhile cross’d with doubt —is it not so?—tell
me,
Lest unequal powers o’ the mind, or slighter
Lore of books, one yoke-fellow might from other
Part but a hand-breadth?
Nay, live thou thy life and fulfil thy sweetness,
Thine, not mine. Fear nought; neither grieve
for fleeting
Time, whoso footprints wear butadeeper channel,
Dear, for my heart’s love.
A. E.W.

Out of nine little children on board the
Wairarapa, not one was saved. Also, their
mothers, in every ease, were drowned along with
them. The first two boats which got away carried
55 people, and only five of them were females.
The proportion of men among the rescued is more
tinn twice as large as that of children and women.
Altogether, it seems to have been an occasion
when almost everyone looked out for himself.
Sundry weird stories are told about the conduct
of the saved. One of them relates to a woman
who got ashore in a state of absolute nakedness,
and to a passenger with two overcoats on, who
demurred considerably about lending one of them
to a nude fellow-creature. Other fully-dressed
passengers are said to have acted in a similar
fashion. Altogether, there are, so far, very few
redeeming features about the tale of the Wai-
rarapa. # , »
Several persons who had a friend among
the drowned passengers of the Wairarapa first
heard of their loss through the arrival of a good
clergyman, who came with a lugubrious expres-
sion to wail over the sad event. And in at least
one instance, when the ghostly comforter had just
scared a woman almost to death with the news
that some relative or other was among the lost, a
telegram arrived stating that he was safe and
well. One man, whose son was on the wrecked
vessel, concealed the news of the disaster from
his invalid wife until more definite intelligence
would arrive, and was under the painful neces-
sity of personally shoving his ghostly comforter
off the premises, so anxious was that good man to
burst in on the sick woman with the news that
her son was drowned, and to pray for her. Also,
he had to intercept and drive away six or seven
religious old ladies who were all arriving in hot
haste to kill the bereaved mother with the intel-
ligence. As it happened, the presumably dead
man was alive and uninjured, but the incident is
instructive, as showing the inherent ghoulsome-
ness of the ghostly comforter and his tribe.
« * *
The evidence in re the Wairarapa wreck
proves, among other things, that not one person
in a hundred or more can form any reliable esti-
mate of time, even under ordinary circumstances.
And the man who has just been thrown out of his
bunk by a collision, and has stove his head against
the floor, and then has scrambled on deck in the
inky darkness to find the ship a wreck, can no
more estimate time than he can eternity. _ Most
of the passengers and crew, in their evidence,
attempted to form an opinion as to the number of
minutes which elapsed between different events,
and succeeded very badly. One, who seemed
more confident than the others, held that it
was fifteen minutes after the original dis-
aster when the vessel listed. “ How long
have you been sitting in that chair giving
evidence ? ” asked the examining barrister. “Ten
minutes,’’ was the answer. It proved to be nearly
half-an-hour, and then the testifier retired dis-
comfited. The average witness’s estimate of time
is built very much on the plan of that Biblical
chronology which says that A bsolom rebelled for
forty years, and that Ezekiel lay upon his right
side for 390 days. He only knows that he seemed
to have been hanging on to the same piece of
wreckage for eleven weeks or so, and from that
inward feeling he vaguely deduces that he was
there any time from three minutes up to two hours
or a month and a half.
• « •
Somebody—apparently a relative of one
of the Wairarapa victims —writes to a Maoriland
paper, asking that the reporters should suppress
the ghastly items concerning how the sharks ate
great chunks out of the drowned passengers. In
the case of the girl who came ashore naked with
the calf of one leg missing and most of the flesh
torn off her ribs, these details of mutilation, he
points out, don’t help the identification in the
least, and are mere superfluous horrors to add to
the distress of her family. All which is doubtless
logical enough, but then a newspaper is a com-
mercial speculation, and the gnawed girl who
came ashore in the daybreak helps to boom the
circulation, and you might as well try to choke a
tiger off its dinner as to choke an editor off such a
narrative.
Maorilanders are now reflecting on a
ouestion asked of the third officer at the enquiry :
“Had the captain orders to make Auckland
quickly and take passengers down to the N-Z.
Cup meeting?” The reply was that he didn t
think so—they had plenty of time, anyway.
• * *
The half-masted-flag-flying foolery during
the protracted funeral of the Czar was run mad
down in Melbourne. Not only did every public
building fly flags for the fortnight, but so did hun-
dreds of stores, pubs., and suburban sheds, ine
storm “ Hoi ” reduced the flags to shrews, but the
Govt, buildings kept on renewing them day by
day. The worst example of the epidemic was the
display of half-masted bunting on Melb. cricket
ground during the match —as if the embalmed em-
peror had been a crack bowler or a patron of the
M.O.C. It is things like this that make the Max
O’Rells of the world laugh at us.|
• • •
The Nihilist boast that the dead Czar was
phosphorus-poisoned is not utterly preposterous.
The symptoms of his illness happen to be identical
with those of such poisoning, which exhibits itself
in a general disorder of all the organs, especially
the kidneys.
NIHILIST CHORUS.
The tyrant of the Bosphorus
Is not the boss for us—
The boss for us;
To date he has been prosperous,
But finds his match in phosphorus—
In phosphorus.

Elijah Cockroft, hanged at Ballarat the
other day for the murder of Fanny Mott, was
unable to read or write, and savagely ignorant.
“A lasting disgrace to the State,” says Canon
Morris, the parson who directed him to Abra-
ham’s bosom. But Cockroft must have dodged
the State’s Compulsory Education Act in order to
preserve his ignorance, and at least the latter does
not send missionaries with pants for the Papuans.
What did the Church do for Cockroft, anyhow ?
Dear Bulletin,—About
gormandisers again. There is a
police official up South, an over-
six-footer, about 18st. in weight
(more or less), and with a fierce
(warranted or unwarranted) re-
putation as a bruiser, one of
whose favorite light luncheons
is—(l) a whole sucking-pig, with
vegetables and bread to match ;
(2) 41b. blancmange; both
courses being washed down with
the contents of two bottles of
pigstout poured into a quart
pot.—Yours, D.
A certain gallant dignitary,
who has just been called on to
explain what is prima-facie a
very ugly piece of sleight-of-
hand over a share-deal, has long
been famous for his aversion to
“nobodies,” which aversion was
much strengthened by an inci-
dent of a few months ago. A
rascally pressman, aware of the
gallant one’s peculiarity, hap-
pened to strike the same rail-
way-carriage as the “majaw,”
who became chatty, mistook the
scribe for a man travelling for
pleasure, don’tcherknow, and
even invited him to call. Just
before, parting the conversation
turned on the state of business
in London, whereupon the news-
paper man incidentally re-
marked that when he was in
London with a whelk-stall he
could not sell “ two hobs’” worth
a night latterly, whilst two years
before he could “ do in ” at least
10s. worth. The major’s face
was a study. He replied ne’er a
word, hurried off and explained
to a crony the same night that
he’d had “a demned narrow
escape. Had chatted with a
fellow I took to be a gentleman
for over an hour, my dear sir !
Got quite familiar with the fel-
low indeed, and, by gad, sir,
what d’ye think he was? A
demned common fish-hawker ! a
costermonger, by gad ! It’s a
lesson to me. I’ll never con-
verse with a strange travelling
companion again.”

from Cooktown the other day.

A belief in spookism is usually unaccom-
panied by a sense of humor, yet a quartette of
Melb.’s boss spiritualists are probably the most
inveterate adult practical jokers in the province.
Last Christmas they landed with their respective
spouses at a seaside hotel just in time for (imner,
Sitting at the table d’hote the men started address-
ing the ladies present by each other’s names, Mrs.
A becoming Mrs. B, and so on. Hence when
Mr. A was found occupying the same room as Mrs.
B the Social Gimletists of the hostelry held a
mass meeting on the verandah and deputationised
the landlord to cast the infamous eight into the
darkness. The situation was gradually unravelled,
to the discomfiture of the long-lipped puritans.
• - * *
The Mellonites have burst up Australian
spookism. Melinite has burst up many things.
#* * .
A deputation of Mormons waited upon
Minister Peacock, at Melbourne, the other day,
to complain because a school-teacher had been
censured for endeavoring to make Latter Day
Saints of State school scholars, and to request the
removal of the restriction in this case. Lity
the Minister could not see his way to adopt the
suggestion of the deputation. At a time when
Woman wants to know why Man doesn t marry
it seems only right that the few amongst the rising
generation who will be disposed to marriage
should be instructed how to make themselves go
as far as possible. It is true that some Victorians,
more or‘less prominent, do their very best to set
themselves up as examples in this respect, but a
few Mormon evangelists in the Education Depart-
ment, if given a free hand, might work wonders
in abolishing the surplus spinsters, and making
the limited supply of husbands go round.

THE JURY'S RIDER.
There crossed the plains last winter, ’mongst hordes of idle
men,
A weary trav’ller worn down very fine,
Whose raes were held together
By a saddle-strap of leather,
With the buckle almost grating on his spine.
For months he’d been existing on the pannikin of flour
The squatter gives (the measure of his sense
Of his duty to his neighbour),
That the man who hawks his labour
May live to crawl beyond the bound’ry-fence.
He was daily getting weaker, yet he gamely struggled on,
Though as slender and as hollow as a reed ;
How he wished the fates would will it
That he’d get a decent billet.
And his stomach be astonished with a feed.
One cold and frosty morning some horsemen, riding out
(They were must’ring lambs for marking) on the plains,
In a paddock, bleak and hilly,
Found a tattered swag and billy,
And this wretched, ragged traveller’s remains.
And they held a sort of inquest on the body at the shed,
With a jury of men working on the run ;
And their verdict, ere they parted,
Was—“ The man died broken-hearted,”
For the ration-bags were empty, every one.
But they added as a rider that “ There must be something
wrong
When working men are travelling in rags
Where endless wool is growing,
And, while flocks of sheep are going
To the * boiling-down,’ men die with empty bags.’
I am Tired!
Sufferers from indigestion and liver complaint are
subject to a continued feeling of drowsiness,
headache, tired, aching limbs, and a general
inclination to languor. These symptoms are due
primarily to a deficient secretion of bile, the
natural cathartic of the liver, which, when secreted
in insufficient quantities, permits the coarse and
fibrous particles of the food and other waste and
poisonous matter from all parts of the system to
become impacted in the duodenum and
intestines, creating a very dangerous condition
which requires instant and proper treatment. An
experience of five years with Clements Tonic has
proved this remedy a specific for such complaints,
and its universal success has resulted in thousands
of imitations being forced on the market; not
because they possess any therapeutic value, for
they are purely speculative ventures. Sufferers
who desire to get relief must insist on having
Clements Tonic. It acts scientifically, has proved
its efficacy irt thousands of cases as effectually as
in that of Mrs. W. Kelhmd, Bega, N.S.W., who
writes on April 26th, 1893 About two years ago
I don’t believe there was a greater sufferer in the
world from neuralgia ; I believe I tried a hundred
different remedies—in fact, everything that was
recommended to me, but without being relieved.
My attention was drawn to Clements Tonic and
the number of cases that had been cured by it—
cases resembling mine. By the time I had finished
three bottles of Clements Tonic I was thoroughly
cured of all neuralgic symptoms, and I have never
had a recurrence of the old complaint. _ I have
recommended it on all occasions. Hoping this
may benefit others who are suffering from the
above complaint.—l am, yours gratefully, Mrs.
W. Kellond, Bega, N.S.W.

interest up to the time of such sale.

beth-street, Sydney.

CHARLES PINHEY, Registrar-General.

be granted.
LINDSAY THOMPSON,

Five dock municipaltty.-re-ad-
JUSTMENT OF WARDS.
His Excellency the Governor, with the advice of
the Executive Council, directs the publication, in
accordance with the “Municipalities Act of 1867,”
of the substance and prayer of a Petition from the
Council of the Municipal District of Five Dock,
representing that the population of that Munici-
pality is now over 1000, and praying for a re-ad-
justment of Wards.
JAMES N. BRUNKER.
The Petitioners state that the Municipal Dis-
trict of Five Dock is at present divided into two
Wards and returns six Aldermen.
That the population now exceeds one thousand,
and consequently the Municipality is entitled to
be divided into three Wards and return nine
Aldermen.
“ Your Petitioners, therefore, humbly pray that
your Excellency will cause the Petition to be dealt
with in accordance with the terms of the said
Municipalities Act, and that such steps as may be
deemed necessary may be taken to increase the
number of Aldermen from six to nine, and that a
re-adjustment of the Wards may be made so that
there be three Wards instead of two as at present,
with a view to the same taking effect at the annual
Municipal elections in February, 1895.” 1
For description see supplement Government
of the 7th Nor., 1894.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Another Y.R.O. Cup meeting has come to a bad
end, as far as the publio are concerned. Once more the
least intelligent of holiday backers, looking at the
tangled results of four days' racing, ask themselves
what is to be gathered from it all, and again the still,
small voice of Reason tells them to cease patronising a
gambling entertainment which gives little or no satis-
faction for the money. A seasoned punter doesn’t grieve
over the mere fact of losing at the race, any more than he
objects to paying £1 for a sweep-ticket, which is only
worth 18s. on Taltersall’s own showing. But he objects
to being beaten, time after lime, by circumstances which
cannot be reconciled with the run of respectable racing
11 luck.”
Anyone who cares to waste time in analysing
“ form ” (not forgetting to allow for fair chances and
mischances in the game) at the late Flemington and Caul-
field meetings, and who compares inconsistent perform-
ances at those places with equally' in-and-out running at
Randwick in September, cannot help thinking that
“sport” so destitute of sporting interest must either
amend its ways or perish of public disgust. Glaring
chicanery is not alone responsible for the present condi-
tion of Turf things. Nor is the dearth of capable jockeys
a principal cause for complaint.
The practice of running good horses when they
are not thoroughly well, and not “fancied by the stable,”
is perhaps more disastrous to the public than even the
loading-up of fields with notorious “stiff ’uns.” It is a
practice which enables Ikey to profit by the least foolish
of all punting arguments, viz., the argument that a first-
class performer is always worth consideration. Camoola,
the Derby and Champion winner of '92, was run several
times at Flemington last year when he was quite unfit to
be put before the sporting publio as a reliable candi-
date. Carnage, the “ crack ” three-year-old of 13 months
ago, was sent for this year’s Cup, and again for the
Canterbury Plate, when his owner admittedly considered
him a weak reproduction of the Carnage of old. Even
the “Master of St. Albans,” a straight owner and an
undoubted pattern to the hulk of his fraternity, is not so
much of a sportsman as to be jealous of his horse’s
reputation. The English turf has been kept going all
these years by the class of rich owners who never run
their “ cracks ” unless they are deemed likely to do them-
selves credit. If a Common, or Isinglass, or a Ladas gets
palpably “out of form” he isn’t allowed to face the
almost certain prospect of an ignominious defeat. An
English racing calendar contains a good deal of respect-
able reading, which explains why a Prime Minister is not
ashamed to maintain a connection with the turf. An
Australian calendar, on the other hand, is a book of
ghastly jokes played upon credulous gamblers.
Harvester, Devon, Bruin, and one or two other
of the older horses have performed with reasonable con-
sistency during the late Canlfield-Flemington season. As
for the rest, the betting at Flemington goes to show that
their relative trustworthiness is an impossible matter for
calculation. The average starting price of the winner in
14 handicap races was 10J to 1, 8 of the minor winners
being returned at the stock quotation of “tens,” which
means that the books could have well afforded to lay 20
to 1 against. Not one absolutely first favorite won a
handicap at the whole meeting. In 10 weight-for-age
races the average starling price of the winner was exactly
sto 1. Fortunatus, who started 16 to 1 in a field of six
horses, gave a big lift to this average, but, leaving his
race out of consideraiion,-we have still plenty of evidence
that an Australian punter stands at fully five times the
disadvantage of an English ditto. For whereas in Aus-
tralia the average price against w'eight-for-age starters is
preposterously short, the average price against the winner
shows that the proportion of good horses which run up to
“form ” is preposterously small.

In re the assignment of Hon. Donald Wallace’s
estate; the first thought of Australian racing men is
what will become of Carbine? The money market is so
tight in Australia, and outside markets will make such
high bids for such a sire, that it looks as if the mighty
one will become an exile.

Several backers of Arihi for the Maribyrnong
complain bitterly of the cowardice of trie V.R.C. in
declining to make certain bookmakers pay up. One man,
on whose ticket an A, followed by the usual blue wavy
line, was scrawled, applied after the race, but was refused
on the ground that he had backed one of the other A-
initialled nags. He appealed to the Club to enforce its
rules, with the result that, although its own detective
reported against the melallician, the club decided that
the amount must remain unpaid.

Carnage, who absolutely refused to race on the
first couple of days at Flemington, was persuaded to
show a bit of his old form in the Canterbury Plate on
Steeplechase day. Gough Hogged him from the jump,
and though he was very much in the rear for a mile and a
half, he began to take an interest in things in the last
half-mile and finished second after a brilliant run.

After Melb. Cup, there was the usual contingent

a breakdown ; and Dawes was trying his best to get his
engagement for Patron cancelled so that he could ride
Tarooola, as, on their track work (Patron, Tarcoola and
Ruenalf worked togeiher), he didn’t fancy the ultimate
winner had a possible chance.

Dear Bulletin, —A record! Mr. Marquess,
sec. for the recently-formed Wyalong Proprietary Racing
Club, acted as secretary, handicapped the horses in each
event (11 items), and on the second day acted as starter,
and sold the winning horses in each cf the Selling-races.
Likewise, he is sole-owner of the course, and had it fit for
racing in a month after taking the matter in hand. —
Yours, WVALONGITB.

* • • •
N.S.W. Postmaster-General Cook has no moral
right to deprive even a bookmaker of an important por-
tion of his civil rights, the use of the post and the tele-
graph-wire, simply because that bookmaker breaks the
law. If a place is kept for gambling purposes the police
have power to practically shut it up. The fact that the
police are too lazy or too corrupt to apply the law
directly, in no wise justifies Mr. Cook’s indirect measures.

beaten, however, by Olem. Hill, who last year hit up 360,
not oat, for the lame college, and under similar condition*.

Both boys, for they are little more, were included in the
S.A. team which met Stoddart’s Eleven. Darling has
done good service in club and interprovincial matches
since he left college, and in a recent interview, published
in the Review of Reviews for October, George Giffen,
when asked to mention some of the most promising
playetsin his own locale, spoke thus of him: —“Darling
is one of the most promising bats we have here. He is a
7ery reliable field, and has plenty of pluck and punish-
in*' power when he gets the bat in his hand.
Mr. Darling’s father, who thinks more of business than of
cricket, lately set up his son as a farmer in the Northern
areas near Port Pirie, but the youth tired of hum-drum
agriculture and yearned for the of the city
and the opportunities to distinguish himself in Us sports.
Two seasons ago, therefore, he left the wheat for the wil-
low and opened a shop for the sale of cricketing material
in the metropolis, having A. S. Waldron, a veteran of the
game, as his partner. He played for North Adelaide club
last year, but has now, like George Giffen, gone over to
the Adelaides. Darling is about 24 years old.
* * * *
If the Richardson-Lockwood lightning bowling
is to be impotent, as Blackham predicted, on hard, true
Australian wickets, the English team can hang their
colors half-mast and prepare to accept defeat with a good
grace. Their fielding at Adelaide was much criticised.
Bad fielding must deepen the failure of the fast bowling.
As batsmen they plaved up to their true form in the first
match-one very big score, another very moderate one—
and on the English average of their chief hope, Richard-
son, they could have won the match. If he is a vain
hope under the altered circumstances—well, where are
they? They dunuo where they are. Until last week an
Australian team had not won a match in which the tall
Surrey man opposed them.
• » * *
Stoddart’s Eleven seems built for sensations.
M’Laren, after his omission from the Adelaide match, was
regarded as one of the “odd men,” and the Melb. papers
agreed in ignoring his pretensions. The Age thought he
would probably stand out of the game with Victoria, and
the Argus found fault with his impulsiveness at practice.
At 12 o’clock, however, M’Laren w’ent first to the wickets
with Ward, and there he remained. The rash amateur,
who was to have stood out, stood most decidedly in. He
appeared on this form the safest and at the same time the
most attractive batsman who has visited Australia since
ever so long ago. M’Laren, against good bowling, may
show plenty of weak points. Against the Victorian
attack he was just immense, slapping and glancing the
ball to the boundary, first on one side of the wicket, then
on the other, and never lifting it at any time in the direc-
tion of a fieldsman. The cheering when he reached his
second hundred couldn’t have been heartier for a local
favorite in the same position.
Just after M’Laren had turned his 150 it looked
long odds against him lasting into the second “century."
Ha was evidently feeling that he had enough of it. Dur-
ing the next quarter of an hour he gave one or two haif-
ohances, and made several questionable strokes. Then
Johnny Briggs brought out another drink to the Lan-
cashire lad, which gave him a'new lease of life. M’Laren
had several drinks through the bleak afternoon, and the
crowd wondered what sort of “tack” he could be par-
taking so freely of.
South Australia’s defeat of England is in reality
a triumph for Geo. Giffen, Little Briggs dramatically
affirmed—at the close of the English second innings—that
Giflen is the greatest cricketer the world has seen, and
the record of the match seems to prove it. Giffen was
very unwell for a week before the match, and was in the
doctor’s hands up to commencing day. The Englishmen
were very down in the mouth over their defeat. S.A. has
a splendid team this year, and on her victory against
England—without a vestige of good luck—how can the
claims of Q. Giffen, Reedman, Lyons, Darling and A. H.
Jarvis—whose wicketkeeping was superb—be ignored
when picking the combined team?

* • • *
So far as England v. S, Australia is concerned,
the writer, who saw all the match, is satisfied of several
things. Jones is a faster bowler than Richardson, and,
although not so uniformly straight, is just as good and at
times better. On a good wicket Richardson gets no work
whatever on the ball. Secondly, Stoddart’s best bowlers
on Australian wickets are undoubtedly Peel and Briggs.
Humphreys will hardly earn his tucker. Thirdly, the
pres nt team is not as good as Grace’s crowd, and Aus-
tralia ought to win three of the five test matches, all the
luck being equal. Again, Stoddart’s men do not field as
well as previous teams, nor does Gay fill M’Gregor’s place
behind the sticks.

* • • •
If Humphreys is to be included in the English
Eleven for the test-matches (by desire of the Australian
authorities who are responsible for his coming here at
all), Stoddart will not let the old 'un bowl much, The
Bulletin imagines. Humphreys, on an ordinary Aus-
tralian wicket, is a farce. He can’t bowl them out, and
they needn’t get caught off him if they are content to
smack his lobs with caution. If they do chance to get
caught, the joy has been purchased at a ridiculously high
pri -e in runs, and whether they do, or don’t, the on-
lookers have only one opinion about Humphreys, viz.,
that he’s an absurd item in the game. His failure as a
bowler is nothing compared with his failure as an attrac-
tion for the multitude. Before the tour is through the
multitude will show themselves heartily sick of Sussex
“ lobs.” Meanwhile, which useful mau is to stand out of
the eleven in order that Humphreys may be much worse
than useless in his place ?
• * • •
Briggs has the soul of a comedian, and is per-
petually playing to the gallery— stumping a hundred
Imaginary wickets at the end of a critical run, &c. A bit
Of his real cricket on Saturday against Vic. was, excusably
enough, treated as a joke. He was bowling to H. Trott,
who got the wood on one ball, returning it smartly down
the wickets. Briggs not Only fielded it, but sent it back
like a flash of lightning to the backstop. If Trott had
stirred out of his crease he would have been numbered with
the dead men. Meanwhile the recoil of his smart piece of
fielding left Briggs seated on the turf, where he remained
ft space while the ground rang with laughter and cheers.
* • * •
Humphreys, the lob-bowler, is certainly a start-
ling novelty. He sends five men to the chains, where
they stand like sentries to challenge fours. He leaves one
man in the off, at cover point, and the only man in his
conventional place is the backstop. There is no slip, or
cut, or point, and, of course, no long-stop. When he has
turned the off-theory topsy-turvy, Humphreys takes off
his “sweater ” and hands it to the umpire. As he walks
away to get his run for the first bowl, one notices that
he has quite the gait of an old man. His first delivery
in the Vic. match was to Graham, and made the crowd
laugh, it looked so childlike and bland, but it absolutely
bewildered Graham. The second ball resulted likewise.
The third ball Graham smote mightilv, to long field, for
!• The sentries guarded the fence efficiently. All the
afternoon there was only one “ four ” off Humphreys—
■truck by Laver. Laver tried to give an encore, but a
•entry caught him out.
“On paper,” the N.S. Wales team picked to
tneet the Britishers on Friday is the weakest the province
has put into the field for many a year. Even allowing for
the poor material at hand, the selection people have not
made the most of it.
Turner has not “ come off ” with either bat or
ball for some time past, and should have stood aside for a
good bowler—say, Trickett, Bush, or Hogan. On a sticky
wicket Turner would, no doubt, be well enough, but th‘
is not the time to wager on getting sticky wickets.
W. Richardson, who was not picked in the
original 24 for practice, but was included in the 11 at the
last moment, took the huff and will not play. His per-
formance in taking nine of the powerful Paddington
wickets was, no doubt, the reason of his eleventh-hour
picking, but if the committee are going on those lines an
effort should have been made to get Alec. Bannerman,
who has been playing up to his old form recently.
Given a good wicket, “on paper” the durned
Britishers should stay in for a week against the Sydney
Eleven. However, paper isn’t always cricket.

M‘Laren, in the English-Vict. match, was at
the wickets 5J hours for his 228 runs. Murdoch’s famous
score of 321 was made against Victoria and occupied 10
hours; Moses, also against Victoria, took a similar time
to compile his 297 not out. M’Laren thus scored 9 runs
per hour faster than Murdoch or Moses, but he did not
approach Mac Donnell’s magnificent 239 in 4 hours. Still
further is he behind the brilliant Massie, who, playing
against Oxford and Cambridge Universities, and scoring
206 out of 265, drove up his second century while his
companions pottered up a feeble 12.

The Vic. Amateur Champion Sculling-race,
rowed on the Lower Yarra course, 3 miles 330yds., last
Saturday, brought five competitors to the post, all vastly
inferior to the standard of the past half-dozen seasons.
The winner turned up in Wally Dawson, of Ballarat R.C.,
No. 7 in the Wendouree’s ’93 Champion Eight, who sculled
home twenty lengths ahead of W. Hamilton (Civil-service).
The other three were unplaced, so the race, as may be
guessed, was a miserable fiasco from a spectator’s point
of view. Last year the championship was won by Jack
Fitzgerald, who did the unique performance of carrying
off a champion scuiling-raoe in the morning and two
cycling-races in the afternoon.
