Every sport has its day. The popularity of
Australian cricket declined; then boat-pulling was on top.
It, too, went down, and footracing got a short turn.
That went'to the dogs, and boxing was taken up for a
year or so. That has gone down, and now cricket has an
innings, with slight signs of a revival in pedestrianism.
The turf has a permanent place in popular favor, and foot-
ball, being a winter game, has no outdoor rival.
Dear Bulletin, —You allow a pote to sing a
hymn of praise to a certain young lady’s legs in your
Poverty Point col. A poem on the late Tommy Corrigan’s
legs would come gracefully from the same pen.—Yours,
Fair Play.
Unique sporting case from Hawkes Bay (M.L.).
A horse won an event at a trotting meeting, and the
result-board on the course and the daily papers, besides
placing him first, gave the names of two others as second
and third. In trotting-law if a horse “distances” his
field, i.e., wins by so many yards he gets first and second
money, and there is no second or third “ placed.” In
this case the first horse was objected to as being over the
measurement, and on the stewards measuring him, they
found it was so, and disqualified him. Then the owner of
the second horse applied for the stakes, but the stewards
refused to pay on the ground that the judge had not
placed a second horse. The Owner sued, but in court it
was ruled that as one of the racing rules said “The
decision of the stewards shall be final,” there was no
appeal. Club-footed justice with a vengeance I

The treatment of Piles by the new Oannalin
remedies is necessitating the dispatch of large
quantities to all parts of the colonies. Applica-
tions must be made direct to the depot at 257
George-street, Sydney, N.S.W., where a copy
of Mr. Sharp’s popular work on “ Piles ” can be
obtained free.

The amenities of journalism. The Age
referred to a recent Argus utterance, wherein
Premier Turner was called “anamiable Brigand,
as the scream of “ the Oollins-street bunyip.”
Redmond Barry, the Victorian J udge
who shot Ned Kelly’s bolt, used to say that
the Australian slang word “ shout” was derived
from the expression “paying the shot.’’ But it
more probably owes its origin simply to the term
“shouting,” in the sense of “calling,” for drinks.
Perhaps some Bulletin reader can improve on
both theories ?
“ As a precaution against violence on the
part of those in authority, the people, both in
England and the United States, have set their
faces sternly against the creation of large standing
armies.”—Age. “ There is no harm in the wheels
of political intercourse being thus lubricated, pro-
vided that we build two cruisers to one of our
friends, and, say, three battle-ships to their two.”
Argus, same day, on Rosebery’s diplomatic utter-
ances re the friendly attitude of Russia and
France.
Two agitations are proceeding in the great
Sydney freetrade daily press—one for the training
of Australian youths to a sea-faring life, the other
for the establishment in Australia of a gunpowder
factory. Both movements, from a freetrade point
of view, are simply absurd. We can now get
powder from England far more cheaply than we
can make it, and we can import “ Dutch ” sailors,
not to mention “Dagoes,” at less than half the
wages Australians would expect. Let us stick to
Freetrade —and then, if war comes along and we
have to defend our country, we shall be able to
enlist an army of half-starved rouseabouts and
drinky narrow-chested billiard-room and tote-sbop
spielers, instead of a legion of expert engineers
and other clever artificers. Besides, why can’t we
hire Chinese to fight for us ?

Sydney jobbing-printers’ strike has ended
in the men’s unconditional surrender. The
customers will get the whole benefit of the reduc-
tion, for the masters will now proceed to compete
until they decrease the profit-margin to the
previous level. Then there will be 21 demand for a
farther reduction of wages.
Another white man murdered by Mackay
kanakas ! This is the sixth such murder at Mackay
this year, the previous five having gone unpunished.
The blackbirds have come home to roost, and are
paying off old scores by retail. But it is a comfort
to know that the Mackay sugar-crop is doing
splendidly, and that the two co-operative sugar-
mills will clear this season upwards of A2U,uuu
profit.
Judge Windeyer complained bitterly the
other day about the manner in which a witness
shuffled his feet around while giving his evidence.
He shuffled his feet all the time, but he shuffled
harder than usual under cross-examination, and
from the agitation of his extremities Mr. Winde-
yer concluded that he wasn’t telling the truth.
Under the Irish system a witness is placed on a
table where his legs are in full view of the court,,
and Mr. Windeyer thinks that this idea might
advantageously be introduced into N.S. Wales.
The question arises whether a nervous witness on
a table, knowing that everybody in the court
(including the judge who tried the Mount Rennie
wretches all round the clock) was looking at his
legs (also his neck), and possibly conscious that
they (his legs) were both crooked and that ms
pants were frayed at the ends, would not be liable
to give way to nervousness in any case. Any
ordinary man can be reduced to an agony of
nervous excitement if he knows that a large crowd
is gazing with horrible intentness at his legs.
Very few people’s legs can bear such close inspec-
tion. After all, Charles Lamb was right, ihe
most dangerous rogue is the man who can look yon
straightest in the face.
Sydney Wool Sales.
M’Burney, Wallis & Co. report.—No change in prices
may be quoted for this week. Buyers still have the same
aversion to all heavy, seedy or burry, or roughly-got-up
clips. Light, free, carefully-prepared lots alone command
anything like the brisk competition which has hitherto’
always marked a Sydney wool-sale. We have the con-
solation of knowing the market here is at least equal to
London. With manufacturers in Europe and England at
present overstocked we cannot anticipate a rise in the
near future, and our only advice to country friends is—
sell in Sydney ; and we are glad to note the plucky man-
ner in which the wool-growers of the colony are already
meeting the market. In a season like this the grower
who hampers his agent with a big reserve is certain to
lose a farthing or a halfpenny per pound in the long run.
The first bid is always the highest, and buyers will seldom
repeat it at a second offering, nor will they hold to it
while agents refer to owners. Sydney, Nov. 16,1894.
IMPORTANT NOTICE.
Legal Managers and Secretaries of Mining and
Industrial Companies carrying on business on the
Coolgardie Goldfields are informed that in order
to comply with the “Companies’Act,”of Western
Australia, it is necessary to advertise all notices
of calls, dividends, meetings, applications to
register, &c., in a daily paper published on the
field. The Golden Age is the only daily paper
published at Coolgardie, and the charge for inser-
tion of such advertisements is at the rate of 3s. 6d,
per inch per insertion.

Dear Bulletin, —Your “ Brass talks
par. recalls a speech made by a one-time M.L. A.
for Leichhardt, W.Q., who addressed the Spring-
sure electors thus : “ Gentlemen, all of you know
me pretty well. You know I can’t talk worth a
rap. You know I own a lot of sheep. Sheep
are the backbone of this district. If it wasn’t for
the sheep none of you could live here. I think
I’ve said enough to convince you that you should
put me in.” And they did. —M. R.

Carnage, one may imagine, will go to the stud
shortly. That s where he would like to go, and when a
beautiful horse has his head full of romantic notions he
can’t race any better than a love-sick maid-servant can
scrub the kitchen floor. Carnage has lost all disposition,
if not the power, to gallop as of yore. When the indolent
rascal puts his nose in the bucket he winks his other eye
and drinks to “ the Ladies 1 ”
* • ♦ ♦
Now that Carnage has gone so wrong, young
Wallace is the apple of W. R. Wilson’s eye. He bred the
big, wn iy-che»tnut horse himself, and sings his praises
f.rvently. . . ,

Knowing ones in Melbourne are offering 10 to 1
that they can name the next jock, whom the V.R.O. will
irrevocably wipe out.
* • ♦ •
The exuberant youth who stood his friends a
swell feed on Cup Night, on the strength of his having
named the treble for which the Herald-Standard offered
£25 prize, was a bit disappointed when the editor of that
paper informed him next day that “so far they had not
got halt through examining the coupons, but that up to
then 89 had guessed the treble correctly.”
* • • *
A gentleman-spieler, whose social position has
hitherto enabled him to secure a good living out of rich
“ mugs ” who parted their money to him to invest on un-
doubted “stiffs,” had a bad time lately. He had
backed Fortunatus to win £lOOO for one mug, Regina to
win £3OO for another, and Tiraoni to win £4OO for another.
All, as he thought, hopeless stiffs. As they all came off
and the spieler had not put a copper on any of them he
was taken suddenly ill on Monday. “ Doctor forbids
anyone to see him,” says the spieler’s housemaid. As a
matter of fact he’s now ploughing the briny to Hmgland.
• * • ♦
A lot of bad ill-notes were passed on the
“ books ” on Melb, Cup Day, and it was only just before
the Cup was run that one “ bookie ” tumbled to the fact
that the last three notes he had bet to were “ wrong
’uns ” Luckily he (the “book") knew who had passed
'em and he immediately hunted up over 100 of the same
notes from fellow “books,” and, when the backer turned
up for £lOO (100 to 3, Patron), the “book” handed
over, without comment, the £lOO all in the wrong-’un s
own particular brand of note. The exchange of winks
between “bookie” and backer said more than a 3-vol.
novel — wa s significant enough, indeed, to remind the
backer of a pressing engagement up North, which pre-
cluded his taking further part in the carnival.
• • • •
Dicky Dunn, the well-known Lmdon ready-
money “book,” lately bid £60,000 for Stoodleigh Court,
a lovely place in Devonshire, near Tiverton, at present
occupied by Mr. George Cheeke (son of the late Justice
Cheeke, of N.S.W.). The offer was refused, as the owner
wants £70,000.
* ♦ * *
George Giffen has a high opinion of Richardson,
and places him far above Jones, the S.A. lightning trun-
dler. It is well-known that Giffen was frightened of
Richardson on the English fiery wickets. The latter, by
the way is built to last, and does not exhaust himself
like Jones. He goes list., is 6ft. high, and is an ideal
athlete.

The new style of starting on all-fours, introduced
to Sydney a few years ago by an aboriginal sprinter, now
promises to become universal. In some quarters, runners
are allowed to place their hands over the mark, but thia
is not fair.

Crossland, an English amateur, has run 20 miles
in Ihr. 51m. 5 4-ssecs.
« ♦ ♦ *
When, a dozen years ago, Cortis cycled 20 miles
within th’e hour it was pronounced the athletic feat of the
generation. Latest Eng. papers show that J. A. Robert-
son lately did 26 miles 1670 yards, almost 27 miles, in
60min. The mile record has been lowered to Imin. 60 2-5
secs, —but these improvements are due to the machines
rather than to the riders.
* » * •
There has been a little row amongst some of the
“ fringe ” of London racing society. Alf. Savile, the well-
known bookmaker, and Cockburn, a quondam pal of the
late Abington Baird (who was concerned in a row at that
gentleman’s house in Berkeley-square when Chesterfield
Goode fought Charlie Mitchell with a poker), a few weeks
ago drove to a house in Regent’s Park occupied by a
Yankee named Taylor. In a few minutes Savile was flung
out, cut and bleeding, and a policeman, on entering the
house, found Cockburn lying on the floor, cut and slashed
about fearfully. Two cutlasses were found near him
covered with blood. Cockburn was at once taken to the
hospital, and his life was in danger. What the row was
about no one seems to know.
* * * *
Cycles are not admitted to any portion of the
V.R.C. racecourse. Is it in revenge for the threat of a
prominent cyclist that the Cup would yet be run on i
machines, and the green track give place to an asphalt
one?
• * * •
Cooma Express says that “ the gun which
fatally shot Lord Ancram, near Bombala, two years ago,
also shot Mr. G. Neuss, at Rook Flat, a few weeks since.
When Neuss was shot he let the gun drop, and it tell
among some rocks and a portion of the mechanism was
broken. It was brought to Cooma for repairs, and Mr.
Thompson, who was carrying it home, by some means or
other received a terrible blow on the head from it. Blood
gushed forth from the wound and Mr. Thompson was
rendered almost insensible, he having to obtain medical
aid. The owner of the gun (which is a most valuable
one) has given instructions to have it destroyed.”
• * * *
The cable announces that Eitzsitnmons has
been arrested for alimony at New York. This, probably,
is at the instance of his first wife. Like the music-hall
i comique, the pug., on attaining notoriety and money, too
1 often flings over the wife who has shared his troubles, and
picks up a flash woman, by whom he is deservedly robbed
and deserted when fortune deserts him And now Fitz.
has got into trouble by killing Con. Riordan, the Mel
bourneite, with a blow on the point of the jaw.

TWENTY POUNDS
OB
Twenty Pence .
You’ve twenty pounds to spend—or twenty
pence, if you like, Your object is to get all you
require for the money you have, and you do not
desire even to contemplate the possibility of being
called upon for more. If you BUY OF US you
will get all you determined to secure, and have a
bit left for something eke. Also, you will get the
best of every thing—the goods of standard manu-
facturers. If you BUY ELSEWHERE your
TWENTY POUNDS, or your TWENTY
SHILLINGS, as the case may be, will certainly
not go so far as you thought; you will either have
to SINK THE QUALITY, and be content with
inferior goods, or
Shell out more Cash .
Will that suit you ? We trow not. You’re no
fool. You’ve been wandering up and down this
blessed world for years and know your book.
One may say, “This is only assertion; where’s
your proof?” We reply that even as the quality
of that popular mixture of various compounds and
substances known as a pudding is proved by its
effect upon the constitution of the person who
assimilates it, so are we prepared with sound and
lucid demonstration. We say—Use your eyes,
exercise your judgment, criticise and compare.
Set your mental machinery to work, and convey
to your mind, through the medium of your organs
of visions, and with the aid of those glorious
reasoning powers with which you have been en-
dowed, the impressions that will be yours, and
you’ll find engraved upon your heart of hearts the
name of

The maiden lady who left Lord “ Randy ”
Churchill a large fortune also bequeathed annui-
ties to a number of fat cats. It was evidently
Pussy Churchill's moustache that “fetched ” her.
Dear Bulletin, —There has arrived at our
camp a new-chum Englishman, a real type, don’t-
cherknow. From his own statements he is a
remittance-man —middle-aged, and his wife and
Son (!) have taken charge of his business and sent
him to Australia to lose himself for three years,
and if he manages to live over that period he may
go back again. Meantime, he yearns for “ colonial
experience.” The boss told him off the other day
to cut down a 6in. sapling. He armed himself
with the small-sized cross-cut saw and got a mate
to help him to lay low this mighty monarch of the
forest. “Well, my lord,” said the boss, “I’m
glad to see you taking kindly to the cross-cut; it
will be useful to you when you go out as jackeroo
on a back-blocks station. But you must remem-
ber, my lord, that no one is considered an expert
with that tool until he can use a 6-foot cross-cut
single-handed.” “Weally?” said his lordship,
and he paused awhile. Then : “ But tell me,
don’tcherknow. How do you keep the wetched
instwument from —aw wobbling, don’tcher-
know ?” Well, we don’t know. Do you ? —Yours,
Dipso.
Dear Bulletin, —“ Steele Grey ” is right
re the female aborigine. Here are two cases
still in the flesh. Mary, Queen of King
Neddy (deceased) and of Bonshaw, on the
Dumaresq River, first produced half-castes,
one of whom is, or was, the only half-blood
of this island whom I ever knew to commit
suicide. He killed his mate and shot himself
rather than allow the police to arrest him. The
above lady had afterwards Dinah, Ellen, Herbert,
all full blacks, and is still alive at Texas. Another
case, which is not exactly the same, is that of
Maria, half-caste. She has half-
caste; (2) dirty white; (3) full black; (%) half-
caste ; (5) half-Chinaman, his father being a
Chinese cook. These last have been all gathered
into the Wesleyan fold by baptism about a year
ago, and the N.S.W. Govt, supplies them with
rations and clothing, whilst many white people
starve.— Gonyan Tom, Dumaresq River.

Mitchell’s Cheques .
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
R was Mitchell, of Bogargo, and his fists were
swift and free,
And he had a type of humor which dealt largely
in surprise
When a station-hand requested him for money
owed —then he
Gave him promptly in full settlement a pair of
fine black eyes.
It was Donnelly, the keeper of the pub. at Iron-
bark,
Would observe, at times, with chuckles grim, a
pair of human wrecks
Coming slowly down the dusty road, and then
would he remark—
“Go and close the bar, Maria! Here are two
of Mitchell's cheques.”
It was Thomson who a billet at Bogargo Station
took,
And as nice a little man he was as you would
wish to know;
It was Mrs. Thomson took a situation there as
cook,
And her fighting-weight it was about two
hundred-weight or so.
It was likewise Mr. Mitchell who, when Thomson
sought his cash,
With despatch did add him quickly to his list of
human wrecks ;
It was also Mrs. Thomson who of Mitchell made
a hash,
And from that day forth the spelling changed
of Mister Mitchell’s checks.
Vic.
C.R.
The South African Review takes a front

The Geraldton (N.Q.) editor :
The Salvation Army continue their triumphant course
in Geraldton. Much of the success is due to the engaging
Lieutenant, whose bright eyes, graceful form and excep-
tional musical ability render her so attractive. The Com-
manders who move the Army are discreet in the selection
of their officers, male and female, and in the case of
Geraldton have shown good judgment. We cannot leave
the subject without bearing tribute to the earnestness
and untiring heartiness with which the pretty Lieutenant,
whose name we do not know [what is he giving us I —Ed.
B.], carries out her duties.
She holds the soul of that editor in the hollow of
her hand.
N.S.W. Education Department has re-
turned to the ridiculous vogue of admitting into
public schools babies under five years of age, the
excuse alleged being that, in order to carry out the
arbitrary regulation that there must be a certain
capitation attendance or a school must be closed,
the exclusion of babyhood had resulted in the
closure of many schools. In order to support one
absurd regulation, the department insists upon
adhering to a second absurdity. Why not abolish
the rule that requires an impossible capitation
attendance in sparsely-settled districts? Why
not discriminate between districts ? Because red-
tape is the incurable leprosy of administrative
incapacity.
The halcyon days of the expert shorthand-
writer have gone for ever in Australia. Up to
recently a verbatim reporter charged, and got, a
retaining-fee of two guineas a day, and, in addi-
tion, one shilling for every folio of 72 words re-
ported. Now the Is. a folio has been cut off by
Govt, and the lawyers, who are the chief em-
ployers of shorthand-writers, and the reporter has
to be content with his daily two-guinea fee —the
client paying the typewriter, to whom the record-
ing angel dictates his notes. The other day, when
a shorthand-writer sued a lawyer in a Sydney
court for Is. a folio in addition to this fee, the
lawyer swore that the rate was not a customary
one, and, further, that a Herald reporter had
been working for him at two guineas a day and
only 3d. a folio additional. The fall of the rates
is not due to the competition of women or to the
plentifulness of expert male shorthand-writers.
There are said to be some clever lady phono-
graphers in America, but women have so far been
utter failures as verbatim reporters in Australia,
though they are on the average better typists than
ffien in oases where they have not to work at con-
tinuous high pressure. Nor are there many first-
olass male shorthand-writers available, for not
more than one man in 500 who fags at shorthand
makes an absolutely dependable verbatim writer.
Reliable shorthanders are as scarce as prima-
uonnas—and always will be.

“The dancing-rooms are possibly doing
more barm than the churches.” — Sydney Sunday
Times.
At 75 years old the good, sturdy President
Pretorius, of the Transvaal, thought of marriage
(says the South African Empire), and wrote to a
buxom widow at some distance notifying that he
would call on her with a licence. So one day he
spanned-in his mules, paid £7 10s, for his licence,
and leisurely trekked to the widow’s homestead.
He had lingered too long. “ Jij es toe laat,” said
the widow, “I was married last week.” Pretorius
shook hands, lit his pipe, and returned to
Pretoria. “Mr, Bok,” he said, “ I want you to
scratch out that name and put in Widow .
I think she’ll have me.” “I can’t do that, Mr.
Pretorius, the licence is annulled; but go down
to Oom Paul and see what he can do.” The ex-
President gravely went down to his successor and
stated his case: “No, no; I can’t do as you
wish ; but here is £7 10s. for a new licence.” Off,
then, went the old man to the registrar, secured
his new licence and the widow as well.
HE MIGHT HAVE ESCAPED ANY DAY.
There was once a man, who, on account of his
religious convictions, was arrested by a despotic
government and thrown into prison. Looking at
the strong stone walls that shut him in, he natur-
ally concluded that he must spend the rest of his
days in this lonely and dismal place. Yet wretched
as the prospect was, there was some comfort in the
thought that he was a victim of oppression, and
not undergoing punishment as a violator of any
good and righteous law. All the same, he would
have been glad to get out, as he had a right to do
if he could.
Nevertheless, there he stayed and languished for
sixteen years. At nearly the end of that period
it occurred to him one day to climb up on a
projecting stone in the wall and take a peep
through the window above his head. He did so,
and found the iron bars removable and the sash
not fastened. An idea struck him, of course.
Why not escape ? He opened the window, jumped
six feet to the ground, and left that district
immediately. He might have done so at any time
during his protracted occupancy of the place, if he
had only been blessed with a more inquiring mind
and not taken so much for granted.
Now here comes an incident both like and unlike
that of the martyr ; like it because there was loss
of free action, unlike it because the sufferer was
not imprisoned as he was.
The story is in the form of a letter, one of those
letters we all prefer to get—short and made up of
short words. The writer, a lady, says : “For the
last sixteen years I have been greatly afflicted
with bilious complaints and weakness. I was
always tired, weak, and low-spirited. I had no
appetite, and when food was placed before me
I could not couch it. For days I could scarcely
eat anything. At times I was troubled with
sickness and heaving at the stomach, spitting up a
thick phlegm. I had a troublesome cough, with
pain at my chest and difficulty of breathing. I
got very weak and could scarcely drag myself in
and out of the shop. In December, 1891, I began
to take a medicine I had heard well spoken of by
many of my customers, called Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup. After I had taken only a few
doses I felt relief, and I had not used the medicine
for a fortnight when I felt better than I had done
for years. Thus encouraged, I kept on with it
until I was restored to good health. My son,
Arthur, had suffered off and on for a year from
pain and heaviness after meals, with a sinking
feeling at the pit of the stomach. He was induced
to try Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup, and the
result was the same as in my own case. I know
several persons in this district who have been
cured by the Syrup after all other means had
failed. You are at liberty to publish my letter if
you think it might be of benefit to others afflicted
as I was. —Yours truly, (signed) (Mrs.) M. Ward,
174, Ilkeston-road, Radford, Nottingham, April
Ist, 1892.”
We congratulate Mrs. Ward upon her deliver-
ance from a distressing and dangerous disease.
What a pity she did not sooner learn how easily
and certainly she could be cured. Sixteen years
is too large a slice out of one’s life to be spent in
bodily and mental misery. Very few of us can
have wealth in any case, but health should be a
blessing common and universal as sunshine. And
it might be if everybody knew how to preserve it
when they possess it, or to regain it when lost.
This lady’s disease was of the digestive organs—
indigestion and dyspepsia—the most afflictive and
perilous of all, because it involves every function of
the body, scattering its poison at every point
through the medium of the blood. Nearly all
ailments are but the differently-named results and
symptoms of this prolific parent of evil. That the
remedy she mentions should have cured her will
not surprise those who know its well-founded
reputation. Many worse cases constantly yield
to it.
Our friend the martyr abode in his cell a weary
time because he was ignorant of the simple way
out. If the reader of these lines is a captive to
disease he has no excuse for remaining so. In her
short and honest letter our correspondent points
to the open door.

“The New Woman — A darn to the old
Blue Stocking. ” — Gentlewoman.
In Chicago (U.S. A.) recently a young man
was fined 25d01. and costs for “laughing and hoot-
ing ” at a lady in pants. A lady in pants is a
woman and a brother and must be treated as
such.
Brisbane Worker thus Australianises an
American story:—
A cute Yankee promoter, who thought he understood
the customs of the country, called on the President of a
South American Republic to get a certain concession
signed, “If your Excellency will sign this,” said the
Yankee, “ I am authorised to hand you 50,000 dollars,
and no one will know anything about it.” “Make it
100,000 dollars," replied the President, “and I don’t care
a damn who knows about it.” The deal was done.
That tale appeared originally in an American
magazine, and the President’s answer was, “Make
it 100,000 dollars and tell everybody.” The curse
is simply inserted to make it Australian. No
story is ever properly told in the Australian lan-
guage unless the principal character curses, and
no story has a democratic flavour unless he curses
considerably. Also no story sounds true to the
Australian reader without curses in it. Aus-
tralian is the curse-language of the earth.
The treatment of Piles by the new Cannalin
remedies is necessitating the dispatch of large
quantities to all parts of the colonies. Applica-
tions must be made direct to the depot at 257
George-street, Sydney, N.S.W., where a copy
of Mr. Sharp’s popular work on “ Piles ” can be
obtained free.
The S.M. Herald sneers that the Maori-
land House of Representatives, which it depicts
as “ the Parliament which, above all others, is
supposed to conserve the rights of the poor and
the wronged in the competition of life,” has no
sympathy with horses, because a suggestion about
introducing a Bill to regulate the hours of labor of
“ horses and other animals of burthen ” was re-
ceived with “ loud laughter ” and “ treated as a
joke.” The S.M. Herald thinks this a strange
attitude on the part of a parliament whose statute-
book is supposed to breathe “ the sentiment of
humanity.” It is not strange that the S.M.
Herald, which has nothing but Fat Man sym-
pathy for humanity, can vent big mouthfuls of
pity for horses. The average Tory paper always
did regard the horse as a nobler and more valuable
animal than man. The Fatman bestows care and
attention upon his horse, but calls for the blood,
bones and marrow of the working-man, because
the latter’s name is always Legion.

The Chicago Starch Mills, at Lane Cove
river, Sydney, which were formallystarted the other
day by ex-Premier Dibbs, are another evidence
of the benefits derived from even a small measure
of Protection. Two years ago a duty of Id. per
lb. was levied on imported starch, cornflour,
arrowroot, maizena, and similar articles. In the
case of cornflour a local industry immediately
sprang up, furnishing employment to a consider-
able number of people and distributing a good
amount of money in wages, and, according to the
usual result in such oases, the price of the article
promptly dropped—in this case to about half the
figure at which it stood in the days when the im-
porter had a monopoly of the market. The price
of starch, on the other hand, went up in the
absence of local competition, but with the opening
of the new industry it must also fall according to
the same inevitable law. The owners of the
Chicago Mills, Messrs. Clifford, Love & Co., of
Clarence-street, Sydney, are manufacturers of oat-
meal, arrowroot, cornflour, and many other
articles; coffee-roasters, tea-blenders, &c. They
employ a large number of hands, and distribute
over .£lO,OOO a year in wages, a total which will
be considerably added to if their latest industry
is not blighted in its inception by the importers’
Government, now in power in N.S. Wales, acting
in the interests of the firms which dump down
European produce on Circular Quay.

Extracts from the record-book of Ross
(Tas.) penal station, for 1845 :
Wm. May, No. 882, ship Argyles. Charged by Suptd.
Mr. J. Skeno with insubordination on the 6th day of Feb.,
1846. No evidence—guilty. Sentence—to receive cor-
poral punishment of one hundred lashes.—Signed, Robt.
Bush, Y.M. Benj. Horne, J.P.
Wm. Harvey, No. 2593, ship Canton, 7 years. Charged
by the Suptd., Mr. Skene, with disobedience of orders in
refusing to go out to work with the chain gang on the
19th Dec., 1845. Plea Guilty. Evidence.—Suptd. Skene
being duly sworn, states:—On this morning (he prisoner
came to me some short time before the bell rung for
mustering, and did ask me for some leathers to wear
under his leg-irons. I told him no such things were
issued from the stores, each man in chains provided his
own. He then said he would not go out unless he got
leathers. After the gang had mustered and ready to
march out, Assist.-Suptd. Coles brought the prisoner to
me and told me he had refused to go out to work. I did
then give the prisoner an order to join his party and go
out to work. He told me that he would not. I then
ordered him to confinement. Verdict—guilty. Sentence
—to receive a corporal punishment of thirty-six (36)
lashes.—Signed, L. P. Jones, Y.M., 19th Deo., 1845.
Thomas White, No. 2675, Ship Runnymede, 7 years
transportation in chains, charged on complaint of Mr.
James M‘Namara, H.M. medical officer, with misconduct
in feigning sickness and refusing to go to work and also
with insolence to him on the sth day of August. Evidence
—Dr. James M'Namara being sworn saith on yesterday
morning the prisoner was brought before me. I asked
him what was the matter with him ? He said he had a
pain in his side. I examined him, and was of opinion
that there was nothing the matter with him to prevent
him going to work, upon which I desired him to go. He
then in a very insolent tone and manner called me a
, and said I did not know when a man was sick, and
repeated his insolent language afterwards. Sentence—To
receive a corporal punishment of 36 lashes.—Signed, L.
P. Jones, Y.M. 8 August, 1845.

“Chinese Mary . ”
A STORY OF CHRISTIAN CHARITY
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
; i.
It was on a soft spring evening in Mel-
bourne, sofne two years ago. The moon was full,
and her flooding lustre, in which all things were
drowned, made the city seem as if it were built on
the bed of a wonderful translucent sea. Ear above
the stars shone like lights of ships sailing slowly
on the surface of that sea, and the moon herself
looked like the sun, seen through infinite depths,
of a strange and glorious upper world. The air
was full of subtle scents and whispers wafted
from trees dreaming of summer, and growing as
they dreamt.
• • •
John Avery, storekeeper, of Yandip, was
dimly conscious of these things as he sauntered
down Collins-street. They made him feel in a
vague way that a good Christian, in comfortable
circumstances, might, so long as Providence and
the banks were on his side, live pleasantly enough
even in this sinful world. They even gave him a
faint ghostly suspicion (not then entertained for
the first time) that, possibly, he was better fitted
by nature to be happy amidst such surroundings
than in an awful heaven wherein he should sing
hosannahs, without resting, from everlasting to
everlasting. This old earth of ours is, after all,
a homely and not unjoyful place when one has a
good digestion and a little money coming in regu-
larly.
Yet, although these thoughts passed through his
mind, John Avery was a sincerely religious man
in his own placid, easy-going way, and a better
friend to unlucky, unregenerate persons than
many a loud-protesting Brother, with mouth per-
petually open to proclaim the good tidings of sal-
vation for sinners in the life to come, and purse
chronically lockjawed against appeals for help
made by hungry sinners in this present life.
* « *
Ha had come to Melbourne to spend his
honeymoon, which was, perhaps, another reason
why he felt more than usually in harmony with
the facts of existence. The evening in question
was the last of his visit and the first he had spent
in his own company. He was, of course, fond of
his bride, but the fondness could hardly be ex-
pected to rise to the level of delirium, seeing that
he had married on the quiet grounds that she
would make a good housekeeper and helpmate in
his business. He had had his romance which had
ended in disappointment, and which might have
left lasting bitterness behind, if it had not been
for the natural sweetness of his blood. And,
strangely enough (or possibly not so strangely), it
was of the other figure in this dead and buried
romance that he was thinking as he leisurely
turned into Swanston-street and oame face to face
with a tall dark young woman with painted
cheeks and the unmistakeable air peculiar to mem-
bers of the unfortunate Profession. Changed as
she was, he knew her in a flash, and, in a voice
husky with emotion, exclaimed, “Good God!
Mary, is this you ?”
The young woman looked vacantly at him for a
moment, .and then, with a hard little laugh, re-
plied, “ Yes, John Avery. This is your old sweet-
heart, Mary Allen. Would you like to marry me
nowV’
“ Don’t, for Heaven’s sake, talk like that,
Mary,” he said ; “walk down this way. I want
to speak to you. Do come for the sake of old
times.”
She gazed at him quietly, almost curiously, and
laughed again. But there was no hardness in her
laugh this time. “ The same good old, soft-
hearted John,” she remarked. “Well, 1 will go
with you, but you must promise not to preach to
me. I’m past preaching to.”
» * •
They walked in the direction of Princes
Bridge.
“Aren’t you afraid of Lizzy, John?” she en-
quired suddenly.
“Of Lizzy? No. Why?”
“ Well, you ought to be—and, if she is the same
Lizzy Saunders I used to know, I rather think
you would be if she heard of your parading
through the streets with me, and your honeymoon
not half over yet.”
It was John Avery’s turn to laugh, but there
was a faint ring of uneasiness in his laughter
which did not escape the notice of his companion.
“Oh, yes,” she said, “I heard all about your
marriage. Lizzy’s done a good thing for herself,
and, I daresay, you might have done worse. You
might, for instance, have married me.”
“ Ah, if you’d only listened to me, Mary,” he
murmured, “how different it would have all
been !”
“ Perhaps—but 1 was too ambitious, you know.
God !” she exclaimed, bitterly, “ I brought my
ambition to a nice market.”
They had come to the bridge, and were gazing
over the parapet at the silent river flowing beneath
them.
“Many a night,” she said, “I’ve come here
meaning to make an end of it all down below
there. I’m sure I don’t know why I didn’t. I
suppose I was too great a coward.”
“Poor girl! Poor Mary!” said John Avery,
with a great pity in his voice. And then, in a
hesitating way, avoiding mention of names, he
asked; “Where is he all this time?” “ Hel
The hound !” cried the girl in a tone of passionate
scorn—“ He left me in the midst of my trouble-
left me without a penny and told me I might die
and be damned for all he cared. It died, poor
thing,” she continued gloomily,” but I didn’t. I
only went to the devil.”
" But why,” enquired her old lover, with
grotesque simplicity, “why didn’t you write to
me? You might havo known that I would have
been only too glad to help you.”
“ Ah, yes—l knew, but can’t you think, _ can t
you see, that you would be the last person in the
world I would be likely to write to ?” She looked
gravely at him for a moment or two, and then a
tender, half-humorous smile came to her lips and
made her face beautiful in spite of its coating of
paint. “No, of course, you can’t, poor old John,”
she added, “ and that’s what makes you so good.”
They talked a good deal more after this, and at
last John Avery brought out what was on his
mind. He told Mary Allen that her father and
mother (as far as the latter was concerned John
tampered with the truth slightly) were sorely
anxious that she should come home again. Her
brother Jim had gone to Western Australia, and
her sister Letitia had married Morgan, the local
draper, so that the old couple were living by them-
selves and felt lonely. Would she come? _ She
made no answer for several minutes. Then, giving
her hand to her companion, she said : “ Yes,
John, I will. The life lam leading now is killing
me, body and souL I’ll go home.”
“ Thank God for that,” said John Avery.
11.
Mary Allen had been a week at home
when the Rev. Bolger, the Church of England
minister under whom her parents sat, as the
phrase goes, came, with the best intentions in the
world, to see her and to preach to her a private
sermon. He was a truly good and pious man, but
his eyes were as wide apart as the eyes of a rabbit;
and tact and good taste were to him terms
describing sinful concessions to worldliness. He
had also—intertwisted strangely with a fierce
ascetic purity—a sort of shy pruriency which filled
in with unuttered imaginative questions the
pauses between his spoken moral remarks. He
had only been a year in the parish of Yandip, and
knew nothing of Mary Allen personally. Her
story he had heard, through the medium of his
wife, and, as a matter of course, it lost nothing in
the telling. It takes a good religious woman to
make sharp the angles in the history of another
woman who has gone wrong.
Mary listened quietly to his discourse, but there
was a sombre fire in her eyes which would have
warned a man of this world against saying much
more. The Rev. Bolger, however, was a man of
the next world, and talked accordingly.
“ It must have been a dreadful life for you,” he
said, stroking his weak fair beard with a white
anaemic hand.
“ It was not good,” replied Mary, drily.
“Oh, no—of course not —but now you must
reform and change all that,” he observed, in a
bland, soothing tone.
“ And what do you call what I’m doing now
but reforming, Mr. Bolger?” she enquired, making
a straight line of her lips.
“True enough,” said Mr. Bolger, “but the
question is, my child, are you reforming in a
proper spirit ? That is the vital point. Will you
come to the morning service next Sunday?”
Mary promised that she would do so, and the
clergyman took his leave. She watched him as
he walked down through the front garden, his
long, black shadow casting a gloom upon the
flowers as he passed. And then she resumed her
household duties with a sufficiently heavy heart.
* « •
Her father when she had come back had
taken her in his arms and kissed her. She was
his favorite child, the little one whom he had
nursed on his knees, and to whom he used to go
for comfort when her mother’s tongue was more
than usually sharp. A somewhat weak Rip Van
Winkle kind of man was old Joe Allen, with a
heart bigger than his brain. Her mother was of
a totally different type, a stern, austere woman
whose God was Respectability. She had mani-
fested little surprise, and betrayed no signs of
emotion, when she saw her daughter again. But
justice to her she meant to do, and justice she
would do. And she began doing it by making
her the household drudge, after dismissing the
female servant who cooked for the men employed
on the farm and did all the rough work of the
house. Mary took this as part of her penance
and said nothing. She had one sustaining comfort
in the stolen interviews (they could hardly be
described otherwise) which she had with her
father. Joe Allen, it need scarcely be said, was
not master of his own house.
Mary kept her promise made to the Rev,
Bolger, and went to church on the following Sun-
day. That thoughtful and conscientious clergy-
man preached, for her benefit apparently, a
sermon on the text, “He that is without sin among
you let him first cast a stone at her.”
It was an earnest, impressive, foolish discourse,
full of terrible denunciations alternated with
absurd pathos —the talk, in fact, of a man who
knows nothing of his subject, yet makes himself
believe that he knows all about it, through sheer
power of lungs, and a sense of responsibility to
his audience. And during it all, Mary Allen felt
the eyes of the congregation burning upon her.
But she had more to undergo. When she went
out of the church at the end of the service nobody
appeared to know her. All her old companions
and school-girl friends passed her without recogni-
tion. Honest John Avery, however, leaving his
wife, who went on her way with her virtuous nose
in the air, came up to her as she walked down the
dusty, red road, and said in a husky undertone
“Mary! Mary! Don’t mind him (meaning the
preacher), “he’s a damned fool, and God forgive
me for saying so.”
The girl looked at Avery with eyes full of
wrathful misery.
“ Hadn’t you better go back and ask your
friends why they don’t start to throw stones at
me?” she enquired, bitterly.
That was the last time Mary Allen ever
darkened the doors of a church.
When John Avery rejoined his wife, that
Christian lady remarked, in the tone of sweet
virulence which your very pious woman can some-
times use with such scathing effect, “ So you’re
going to take up with your old sweetheart again,
eh ? I congratulate you on your taste, Mr. Avery.
She has such a nice character—quite virtuous and
respectable. You ought to bring her home with
you while you are about it. You can easily send
me back to my mother, you know,”
All that Avery said in reply was, “ Thank
I’m not a woman—especially a good woman.”
• • *
The measure of loathing with which her
life in Melbourne had inspired Mary Allen was
shown by the unflinching, uncomplaining way in
which she worked out her atonement, which meant
nothing less than a daily martyrdom. Her father
was kind enough to her, in his feeble, furtive
fashion, but her mother gave no sign of relenting
from the stern attitude she had taken up with
regard to her on the first day of her return. Mrs.
Allen was no believer in the virtue of killing the
fatted calf to celebrate the home-coming of
prodigals of either sex. “ A certain fearful look-
ing forward to of judgment and of fiery in-
dignation,” was a text ever uppermost in the
mind of this female Torquemada But though
above the weakness of Mercy, she looked
upon herself as a strictly just woman. And
so Mary was made to feel herself an alien,
living on sufferance, in her own home. When
visitors came to the house —many of the female
portion of them had no other purpose in coming
than to catch a glimpse of her (much in the spirit
in which they would go to see a woman with two
heads, or other monstrosity) in order to be able to
talk about her afterwards—the conversation was
brought to a dead stop if she happened to enter
the room in which they were sitting. This she
never did if she could help it, but kept to herself
in the kitchen on these occasions. Such of the
men-folk as were not absolute brutes behaved
better, and showed a disposition to sympathise
with her in a rough but respectful way, when
their wives were not about. But the members of
her own sex knew how to make up lot this (as
they regarded it) misplaced consideration. If she
went into the township to buy anything in a store,

and there chanced to be any women present at the
time, they made way for her silently, casting sig-
nificant glances at her as they did so. Even the
children were taught to look upon her as a crea-
ture of a lower grade, not fit to be spoken to. The
effects of this lesson were, one day, brought home
in a bitter fashion to the heart of the unhappy
girl. She was in a grocer’s shop making some
purchases, and, seeing a little fellow whom she
had known as a baby standing by the counter, she
patted him on the head and offered him a packet
of lollies. The child looked wistfully at the
packet, but edged away from her, shaking his
head. 44 Mother says you’re a bad woman ’n
I mustn’t speak to you,” he said. This
was nearly the last straw. If they had
only allowed her to make friends of the children—
well, in that case, this story would probably have
never been told. But they thought, so severe was
their virtue, that any approach to familiarity with
ier would contaminate their little ones.
• ’ « •
Her own sister, Letitia, the wife of the
leading draper, who was also a local preacher
gifted with much unction and a powerful exhorter
in his manner, after a frigid acknowledgment of
their relationship, gave her to understand that it
would be better for her not to call at the Drapery
Emporium. 44 We have to consider our cus-
tomers,” said the careful Letitia. Her husband
offered to go to the house and wrestle prayerfully
with the Lord for the benefit of the soul of his
erring sister-in-law, but his erring sister-in-law
would have none of his wrestling. She even so
far forgot herself (some sinners in the place said
it was not without good reason) as to call this
vessel of grace a 44 rancid hypocrite.” Which
brought to a sudden end the previous cold charity
of Letitia Morgan.
• • «
Worse things remain to be told. Yandip
contained the usual proportion of moral Calibans,
with heads hugely developed at the rear, whose
only creed was a profound disbelief in the virtue
of woman and the decency of man. They leered
lasciviously at Mary whenever they met her, and
as soon as they saw that she meant to ignore their
advances, made remarks upon her after the
manner of their kind—a manner which is, un-
fortunately, too familiarly horrible in even the
smallest country town to need description, A
chivalrous local shanty-keeper came to her rescue
on one of these occasions and offered to protect
her from such insults in future—on conditions
which may be guessed, and which she scornfully
rejected, making, thereby, another enemy in the
place.
• • •
The parson’s wife, who considered that
her official position carried with it an odour of
sanctity strong enough to act as a disinfectant
against any conceivable moral contagion, called
upon Mary, and, after preaching a cold little
sermon, in the style of those delivered by her
husband, said that she had heard that Mary was
a good dressmaker. Mary replied with sufficient
meekness that she had that reputation, and her
mother, who was present at the interview,
enlarged upon the subject. The upshot of which
was that the parson’s wife said that she could
obtain some work tor Mary, in the way of making
dresses, from ladies in the neighbourhood, but,
of course, the strictest secrecy would have to be
observed in the unhappy circumstances of the
case. 44 Of course, m’a’am, and very good you are
I’m sure, and very thankful she ought to be for
your kindness,” said Mrs. Allen, speaking on
behalf of her daughter.
The dresses were made and delivered at the
parsonage, according to arrangement. Theunder-
standing was that payment for the work should
also be made in the same conspiratorial fashion,
“What are your charges?” enquired the parson’s
wife.
Mary mentioned them, and the lady threw up
her hands as if astounded.
“Why, young woman,” she said, “you are
charging as much as any dressmaker in the town-
ship.”
“But I am a dressmaker, ma’am,” responded
Mary, “you would hardly have given me the
dresses to make if I hadn’t been.”
“You are far too bold, young woman,” ex-
claimed the parson’s wife with a curling lip, “ and
I regret to observe that you are also ungrateful,
The ladies from whom I obtained this work for
you expected, as I certainly did myself, that, con-
sidering”—with emphasis—“ the peculiar position
you hold, and seeing that the work was given to you
a ? an encouragement, you would not look to get-

With the money she received—it was not

M ary Allen bought some cheap, quiet material
and made a gown for heraelf) not before it was
badly wanted. Her mother watched her, chafing
silently. “Mary,” she said at last, being able to
contain herself no longer, “I thought that when
you gave up your sinful way of living, and came
home, you were done with fineries for the rest of
your life.”
“But, mother,” answered the girl, holding up a
piece of drab-colored print, “you surely don’t call
thi* finery?”
. “ Go your own way, girl,” exclaimed the
implacable woman, “ I wash my hands of you.”
“ God knows,” cried her daughter passionately,
“ I have tried hard enough to go your way,
mother, and instead of helping me, and being
kind to me, you have treated me worse than if I
had been a homeless dog. Your friends have done
the same. If this is what you call Christian
charity, I want no more of it.”
It was, no doubt, a sign of shocking innate
depravity, but the girl had actually grown tired
°l being treated as a social leper—a pariah outside

# * *
It was a cold wintry day when Mary Allen
looked for the last time at the home of her child-
hood; the home in which, since her repentant
return, she had passed six long months of martyr-
dom—in vain.
Her father was standing at the gate of the front
garden. She put her arms around his neck and
kissed him gently, sadly, as a dying mother might
kiss a child she was leaving behind. “Good-bye,
daddy,” she said, “poor daddy! You did what
you could, but everybody was against me.”
“ But where are you going, and what are you
going to do, my dear ? ” enquired the helpless old
man.
44 Never mind. Til get on all right. Maybe
I’m going into a convent to be a nun. Maybe
not. It doesn’t matter, anyhow.” And she
walked away with a light and reckless laugh.
But she turned swiftly back again and embraced
the old man with a fierce fondness that belied her
laughter. And he knew in his heart that the
parting kiss she gave him was the kiss of despair.
When he went indoors he found his wife, her
mouth set firmly, quietly performing her house-
hold duties.
44 So you’ve driven her away at last,” he said,
looking reproachfully at the woman to whom he
had never dared to speak out like a man.
She turned a grey face towards him and replied:
44 Joseph Allen, I have the salvation of my own
soul to look after. Mary has made her own bed ;
let her lie on it.”
And from that day forth, until the day of her
death, the name of her erring daughter never
passed the lips of this rigid Christian mother.
111.
Mary Allen had been lost to Yandip for about a
year when John Avery paid another visit to Mel-
bourne. Business had prospered with him during
this year, and he bad many things to do in the
city. So that it is not particularly strange that
he didn’t find time to think about his old sweet-
heart until the last evening of his stay. This
evening, however, he determined to devote to
making enquiries as to what had become of her.
Luck favoured him. He met, almost at the door
of his hotel, a plain-clothes policeman who had
been stationed at Yandip some years previously,
and who, as a matter of course, knew everybody
living in, or around, that Arcadian village. Roper
was the man’s name, and, in answer to John
Avery’s enquiries about Mary Allen, he replied
with some traces of feeling in his voice— 44 Ah, Mr.
Avery, it’s a sad story. She went to the devil,
at first, as we all know, but she’s gone to the dogs
since. Some 18 months ago, or thereabouts, I
remember she used to belong to the highest set of
women of that kind—you know the kind I mean.
But one day, about that time, she disappeared
suddenly and wasn’t seen in the city again for six
months. She would never tell where she had been
during these six months, but the women who knew
her used to say that she must have been in a
lunatic asylum. If that was so she should never
have been let out of it. She took to drink like a
demon, and not only to drink but to opium, and
sank lower and lower till she could hardly find
even a woman of her own class who would have
anything to say to her. If you wish it, I think I
can show you where she is to be found—but I
warn you that the sight will not be a pleasant
one.”
John Avery said he would take the risk, and
they went on their way.
• « «
Roper led the honest, God-fearing country
storekeeper through reeking lanes and alleys, run-
ning off Little Bourke-street, the like of which he
had never seen before. The policeman turned up
one of the noisomest of these —a dismal alley, the
very stones of which seemed to proclaim it the
abode of crime and misery. Half-way up it there
was a vacant space, upon which was collected a
crowd of the typical denizens of the locality,
cheering and hooting like maniacs. Roper elbowed
his way through them, closely, though reluctantly
followed by John Avery. And then the latter
saw a sight which made him sick with disgust. I n
the middle of a ring formed by the crowd, two
women, with sleeves upturned to the shoulder
bodices torn to ribbons, and dishevelled hair, were
fighting like wild cats. Their faces were streaming
with blood, and they were panting like hounds at
the finish of a chase. The end of the fight was
evidently near at hand. The taller of the two
women, who seemed mad with drink and fury
struck out suddenly and the other woman dropped
like a stone, and lay w here she fell,
44 Good old Chinese Mary !” yelled the crowd.
At this moment a blear-eyed, wizened-faced
Chinaman stepped forward, and, taking the con-
queror in this gruesome combat by the arm, led
her away, staggering and whooping as she went.
44 Have you seen enough ?” said the policeman
to his companion.
John Avery’s face was of the color of ashes.
44 Thank God her poor old father died before he
could hear of this!” he exclaimed, hoarsely.
Eor the frightful creature whom he had seen
led away by the hideous Chinaman was the wreck
of his old sweetheart, Mary Allen.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
“The intoxication of misfortune gives a
man a terrible smile.”
Dear Bulletin,— Ever and anon you jibe
at the immorality of Scotchmen and quote the
illegitimacy statistics. Admitting that the ratio
of illegitimate births in Scotland exceeds that of
other civilised nations, I hold that it may demon-
strate- (1) The innocence of the average Scotch
girl ; and (2) her magnificent healthiness. The
lassie frae Glasgow makes one mistake, and one
mistake owing to her ignorance and her healthi-
ness means one baby. But it never happens again.
When did you last hear of a belle of the pave°with
a Scotch accent? Why, the Australian vital
statistics show that our women are more moral
than the girls from the north o’ the Tweed ? Do
you believe this? —Yours, Thistle.

Subiaco. A baby had been found during the
morning, and some navvies working on the Rose-
hill railway were discussing the event. No. 1
(loq.): “Two or three dawgs in bags have floated
past here lately, and when I saw that bag this

and you’ll catch a lot alongside it.’ I wanted a

but I caught sight of a bit of pink frock or some-

dropped it.” No. 2; 44 Why, you’d a got a quid
for finding it.” No. 1: 44 Yes, and lost three quid
going to the inquest and givin’ evidence when the
poor gel gets tried for it. No, no, Jim, not me.’
